Note on the text
Books, articles and other printed materials are cited in full on first reference, then in shortened form.
Archival materialsâmainly lettersâare referred to by the name of the author and recipient followed by
the date or title, with full citations provided in the Bibliography; sequential quotations from letters
are not indicated by âibid.â Figures are provided in the Appendix. Underlined text in manuscript and
typescript materials has been standardised to italics throughout.

Introduction
âBeing a poet is a form of admitted self-recognition and self-description inside a schedule of
professions and roles within the social categories of a community and the nomenclatures for
operational identityâ âJ.H. Prynne.1
This thesis offers a survey of the context in which the British poet J.H. Prynne began his literary career,
focusing specifically on the period between 1955 and 1975 and seeking to reorient critical attention
towards certain traditions, influences and social and literary milieux which have been overlooked in
scholarship to date. These neglected contexts are then read back into Prynneâs prose work to offer
several new perspectives on his intellectual, political and ethical outlook. Following a very brief review
of existing work on Prynne, this introduction maps the territory which the remainder of the thesis sets
out to explore, explaining both its methodological peculiaritiesâprincipally its reliance on Prynneâs
prose rather than his poetryâand its overall shape.
While Prynneâs poetry has received minor critical attention ever since the publication of his debut
collection, Force of Circumstance and Other Poems, in 1962, serious scholarly discussion can be said to
begin a decade later with Donald Davieâs Thomas Hardy and British Poetry (1972). Here, in what
Keston Sutherland terms âa spirit of literary partisanshipâ, Prynne is inserted by Davieâhis former
supervisor as an undergraduate and briefly as a doctoral student at Jesus College, Cambridgeâinto a
resolutely English poetic tradition.2 Prynneâs work is also used by Veronica Forrest-Thomson in her
posthumously published Poetic Artifice (1978) to explicate a theory of poetic language. A number of
short reviews and studies of individual poems appeared throughout the 1970s, mainly in small venues
such as Prospice and Grosseteste Review, while the publication in 1982 of Prynneâs collected poems
provoked a much wider interest in his work, receiving attention in organs as high-profile as the London
Review of Books. The first book-length study of Prynneâs work, N.H. Reeve and Richard Kerridgeâs
Nearly Too Much: The Poetry of J.H. Prynne, appeared in 1995, following an increasing number
of articles both in âlittle magazinesâ and in mainstream scholarly journals. More recently, Anthony
Mellorsâs Late Modernist Poetics: From Pound to Prynne (2005) includes extended discussion of the
poetry, while Ian Brintonâs essay collections A Manner of Utterance (2009) and For the Future (2016)
collect a number of articles on the same subject. Further analysis is provided by dedicated editions of
the journals Jacket (24, 2002), QUID (17, 2006) and Glossator (2, 2010), while doctoral theses focusing
primarily on Prynne have been produced by N.R. Burrell, D.S. Marriott, Anthony Mellors, Rachel
Campbell-Johnston, Birgitta Johansson, Sutherland, Ryan Dobran and Matthew Hall.3

There are two related issues with the prevailing focus of scholarship on Prynne, which it will be
the task of this thesis to clarify. The first is manifested in a general bias towards text rather than
context. Existing studies of Prynneâs work tend to concentrate very closely on the poetry itself, or on
related theoretical issues, without paying much attention to the historical conditions of its composition,
publication and distribution. To take a prominent example, Reeve and Kerridgeâs Nearly Too Much
consists of four chapters, the first two of which read Prynneâs work through pre-determined literary
concepts (âQuestions of Scaleâ, âLyricismâ), while the third relates the poetry to âtheoryâ as such (Bakhtin,
Kristeva, Lyotard) and the fourth consists of a long reading of the The Oval Window (1983). Mellorsâs
Late Modernist Poetics is similarly theory-driven, its chapters headed by concepts such as shamanism,
obscurity and the uncanny, which structure the subsequent readings of Prynneâs work to the exclusion
of historical detail.4 That the most powerful counterexample is Alex Latterâs recent monograph Late
Modernism and âThe English Intelligencerâ (2015), a book which does not even include an entire chapter
on Prynne, only serves to emphasise the lack of local historical analysis in what has gone before.5
It would obviously be optimistic to expect a large body of rigorously historicist scholarship to have
already emerged around Prynne, when book-length studies of his poetry still number in single figures.
Nevertheless, Prynne is surprisingly ill-served even by basic literary history of a semi-biographical type.
Brintonâs articles for PN Review, which inevitably lack the full apparatus of academic scholarship,
nevertheless remain some of the only pieces actually to pay sustained attention to the publication
contexts of Prynneâs poems.6 The notorious impenetrability of Prynneâs work is no doubt part of the
reason for this situation: it is difficult to illustrate accessible, macro-level historical argument with
poetry when the poetry itself requires extensive explanation and interpretation almost every time it is
used. Yet it is perhaps also the combined result of close personal association between Prynneâs foremost
critics and the poet himselfâprimarily through pedagogical relationships but also in the context of a
relatively close-knit avant-garde poetic communityâand Prynneâs own published comments on the
irrelevance of any criticism involving biography. When in âMental Ears and Poetic Workâ Prynne
the Late Modernist Text: The Theory and Context of J.H. Prynneâs Writings from 1960â1974â (unpublished doctoral

The second major problem with scholarship on Prynne appears as a bias against consideration
of the very earliest stages of his career, and therefore, in combination with the focus on text over
context, against the influences that shaped that part of his career. The precise details of this bias will
be considered in the introduction to Chapter 1, but it is important at this stage to take account of
what it obscures. For many critics of Prynneâs work, his poetic career is taken to begin in earnest with
1968âs Kitchen Poems; that is to say, Prynneâs deliberate suppression of his debut collection, Force
of Circumstance, is accepted relatively unproblematically. Counterintuitively, this would be less of
a problem for Prynne scholarship in general if Force of Circumstance was more similar in style to
the later work. As it stands, the sense that the book is an obvious outlier leads to an unjustifiable
hypostatisation of the break that follows its publication. A seemingly unbreachable wall having been
erected between Force of Circumstance and what follows it, everything associated with the book is left
on the far side, presumed to be irrelevant to the later work. As will be argued in Chapter 1, the fact
that this shift in Prynneâs workâand it is certainly an important shiftâcoincides with his increasing
receptivity to American poetry has the effect of ânationalisingâ the respective parts of his career, with
certain English contexts being ignored, even where they might shed new light on much of what Prynne
was writing throughout the later 1960s and â70s.
In order to counter the two problems identified aboveâa focus on text over context and a reluctance
to consider the earliest phase of Prynneâs careerâthis thesis will concentrate almost exclusively on
Prynneâs prose work rather than his poetry. Such a drastic restriction in scope requires some justification.
It is important to emphasise from the outset that this is primarily a methodological decision rather than
a choice about content. In other words, while certain prose pieces will be read in detail, and their status
as prose made an object of analysis, this is not intended to be a thesis about Prynneâs prose. Rather, it
is a thesis which adopts Prynneâs prose as a toolâone among manyâto explore the broader contexts of
his literary career. There are two characteristics which make the prose a particularly effective tool for
this job. At the most basic level, Prynneâs prose writing is significantly more transparent to contextual
analysis than his poetry. While the style is in some cases just as obscure, this is not usually the
case. Furthermore, Prynneâs prose pieces tend to be tied much more obviously to specific occasions of

Critical discussion of Prynneâs prose work has generally been undertaken as a means of entry to the
poetry. One of the most frequently discussed works is the very early piece âResistance and Difficultyâ,

Recalling one of a number of visits to Ezra Pound during his incarceration at St Elizabethâs Hospital
in the late 1940s, Charles Olson writes that âI asked him if anybody had commented on his prose.
He started out to say plenty, but I saw he meant the subject matter, and I stopped him, and meant
as language, but that was the end of the interview.â16 While the review above is admittedly partial,
focusing on texts which will be considered in the present thesis, it is nevertheless representative enough
to make clear that the situation in Prynneâs case is worse, with very little commentary existing on
either the content or the language of his prose work. While the pieces can be assigned roughly to
the periods of Prynneâs poetic work by their dates of composition, this operation is for the most part
not justified by analysis of the texts themselves. The freedom which this situation allows will be
particularly evident in Chapter 1, in which prose from the early stages of Prynneâs career is discussed
in order to gain a clearer picture of his early influences than that which emerges from the poetry alone.
Through the use both of published pieces and of archival material such as letters, which fall under
this thesisâs definition of prose, an idiosyncratic and specifically British intellectual milieu anchored
by the work of Donald Davie and F.R. Leavis is proposed as an important and hitherto overlooked
context for Prynneâs early work. This context is then connected to a conception of the academy, and
the University of Cambridge in particular, which, it is claimed, extends throughout Prynneâs career.
In the first half of Chapter 2, a similar strategy is used to illuminate the little magazine scene which
served as a conduit for so much of Prynneâs poetry and prose throughout the 1960s and â70s. As in
Chapter 1, attention is paid to activities which blur the boundary between what is generally considered
to be Prynneâs juvenilia and the work of his mature career, while a focus on material practices and
contexts is maintained. The second half of this chapter, by contrast, accepts the textual focus of current
Prynne scholarship, but turns this attitude in the direction of a prose text, undertaking an extended
reading of Prynneâs 1967 piece âA Note on Metalâ and exploring questions about the nature of his prose
work in detail. Finally, Chapter 3 turns to prose fiction, proposing a third key context for Prynneâs
work in the writing of Wyndham Lewis, Edward Upward and Douglas Oliver. These three authors are
claimed to occupy a loose tradition into which Prynne also intervenes with his 1974 text âThe Plant

In-keeping with the largely contextual focus of this thesis, the choice of the period 1955â75 as its
object of study was made by looking simultaneously at the internal contours of Prynneâs career and at
events outside it. The concept of âthe Long Sixtiesâ, defined roughly as the period 1958â74, is familiar
from the work of Arthur Marwick, and just as in Marwickâs model the initial date of this thesisâs
expanded scope is firmer than its endpoint.17 1955 was Prynneâs final year at school; it is also the
date of his earliest recorded poem, and of his first involvement with a little magazine, as editor of the
sixth-form journal which published it, a position which will be examined in Chapter 2.18 In broader
historical terms, any number of factors could be cited to support a case for 1955 as the beginning
of an era in which the cultural emphasis in Britain came to rest firmly on the first rather than the
second part of the phrase âpost-warâ: ten years on from the Second World Warâs formal end, the first
full year without rationing also saw the symbolically important resignation of Winston Churchill as
Prime Minister, to be replaced by Foreign Secretary Anthony Eden. Perhaps more importantly for the
concerns of this thesis, D.J. Enrightâs anthology Poets of the 1950s was published in 1955, solidifying
the poetic infrastructure of âthe Movementâ which, as Chapter 1 will argue, helped to determine the
course of Prynneâs early career.19 As acknowledged above, 1975 is weaker than 1955 as a point of
periodisation; nevertheless, it has some key virtues. According with the terminal point given by Mellors
for âlate modernismâ (1945â1975), it includes Prynneâs significant collection Wound Response (1974)
and its associated prose text, âThe Plant Time Manifold Transcriptsâ, a work which Chapter 3 will
argue is highly representative of tendencies present in other prose fiction published throughout this
period.20 Importantly for the largely British focus of this thesis, 1975 also marks the first appearance
of Prynneâs poetry in a language other than English, with the publication of Bernard Dubourgâs French
translations of Kitchen Poems, Day Light Songs and Fire Lizard.

Inevitably, there are relevant areas of enquiry which, primarily for reasons of space, this thesis
is not able to pursue. The first of these is Prynneâs relation to the concept of the nation in general,
and the United Kingdom in particular. This thesis generally uses the terms âEnglandâ and âEnglishâ
when referring to poets born, living or writing in England and âBritainâ or âBritishâ when referring
to the state, acknowledging the existence of culturally distinct poetic traditions in Scotland, Wales
and Northern Ireland. This is not to deny the many geographic rifts within England as a cultural
entity, but the fact that England is in the present work generally used in broad-brush opposition to the
concept of âAmericaâ justifies a degree of generalisation. In any case, Prynneâs Englishness (though not
necessarily his idea of it) fits for the most part within a Home Counties, Received Pronunciation, public
schoolâOxbridge, BBC model which for the bulk of the period under study here remained culturally
hegemonic. Adding to the pressures against including a detailed study of nationhood in this thesis is
the fact that these issues were considered at length in the authorâs own M.Phil. dissertation, and while
some of its conclusions would be dissented from in the present context, the evidence and analysis on
which they are based would not.22 The other topic largely absent from this thesis is less straightforward.
The study of Prynneâs prose was compared above to the study of Ezra Poundâs, but this is far from
being the only point of connection between the two men. In fact, the figure of Pound could be said
to haunt every one of the following chapters. The vitriolic and polemical tone of Prynneâs critical
writing and correspondence, discussed in relation to Leavis in Chapter 1, has a lineage which could
equallyâperhaps even more justifiablyâbe traced to Pound. Like Pound, Prynne has acted as a sort of
poetic impresario for friends and acquaintances, a set of activities which is considered in Chapter 1 and
the first half of Chapter 2. Even Prynneâs discussion of the relation between abstraction, corruption
and value in âA Note on Metalâ, considered at length in the second half of the latter chapter, has shades
of Poundâs economic thought, and while Chapter 3 does engage briefly with Pound on a direct level,
this is subordinated to a focus on Wyndham Lewis. It would be misleading to claim that Pound is
excluded from the body of this thesis for the same reason that it does not include detailed studies of
Olson and Ed Dornânamely, that he is an American. Poundâs long-term expatriate status would make
such an exclusion problematic, to say the least. The real reason is again one of space. To provide a full
account of Prynneâs relation to Pound would (and perhaps one day will) be the task of an entire thesis,
not merely a section of one. As the subtitle of Mellorsâs Late Modernist Poetics: From Pound to Prynne
indicates, some of this work has already been done, but not to an extent which would prevent other
important material from being crowded out in a work as short as this one. Mellorsâs focus, moreover, is

Chapter 1. âThe contemporary situation here in Englandâ
i. Introduction
When the most basic and accessible introductions to Prynneâs work seek to identify his influences, the two
figures who appear more than any others are the British poet Donald Davie, one of Prynneâs academic
supervisors, and the American poet Charles Olson, with whom Prynne corresponded throughout the
1960s.23 Yet the relationship between the citations is in most cases highly unequal. Prynneâs entry in
The Concise Oxford Companion to English Literature is characteristic: âHis first collection, Force of
Circumstance and Other Poems (1962; later suppressed), bore similarities to Donald Davieâs gently
modernist Movement poetry, but his subsequent involvement in the British Poetry Revival exposed
his work to the legacy of American Objectivism, particularly Charles Olson, and shifted it in a more
experimental direction.â24 Leaving aside the misleading reference to the âBritish Poetry Revivalââa
term coined by Eric Mottram and more often associated with London-based poets involved with the
Poetry Society in the 1970sâthe dual purpose of this sentence, epitomised by the casually parenthetical
â1962; later suppressedâ, is clear: to acknowledge the importance of Davie to Prynneâs early poetry while
cordoning off the later work, safely insulating it from any implication of further influence.25 Unlike
Olson, Davie is only âgentlyâ modernist; Prynne, meanwhile, is a sort of poetic negative who is âexposedâ
to the blinding light from across the Atlantic and thereby fundamentally âshiftedâ, never able to return
to his previous form. Prynneâs implied designation of his debut collection as juvenilia, excluding it
from every volume of his collected poems up to and including the recent third Bloodaxe edition, is in
this way accepted as a factually correct literary-historical judgement rather than a highly contentious
attempt at personal canon-formation, and figures associated with the workânot only Davie, but also
Charles Tomlinsonâare consigned to the same ash heap.26
If basic reference works downplay Davie as an influence, the more sophisticated scholarly perspective
on Prynneâs poetic development moves him even further out of view. Despite the relative infancy of
academic work on Prynne, a number of clear conventions can already be discerned. One of the most
important of these is the view that Prynneâs 1971 collection Brass marks a significant shiftâperhaps
the most significant shiftâin his poetic development. As Sutherland notes in his own 2004 account of

If a reliable account of Prynneâs entire career is eventually to be produced, the bias described here

ii. Donald Davie and Charles Tomlinson
Though this chapter will argue that Donald Davie was an extremely important figure during Prynneâs
early years at Cambridge, he was not a permanent fixture. Prynne matriculated at Jesus College
at the beginning of the 1957â58 academic year, a period in which Davie was a visiting professor at
the University of California. Only the following year did Davie return to Cambridge, where he had
completed his undergraduate studies and Ph.D. in the late 1940s and early â50s respectively, and it was
not until 1959 that he was made a fellow of Gonville and Caius College.35 Alex Latter describes how
Prynneâs transfer to Davieâs supervision was the result of direct lobbying by Prynne and R.F. Langley,
a fellow student on the English course at Jesus and later an important poet in his own right.36 Though
the dates for the move are unclear, it seems likely, given Davieâs academic position and the structure
of the Cambridge English Tripos, that he only supervised Prynne for the third and final year of the
course, known as Part II. Even so, in order to catch Prynneâs attention as a potential supervisor, some
sense of Davieâs basic scholarly and poetic approach must already have filtered down to him. Before
examining Prynneâs recorded comments on Davie, it may therefore be helpful to sketch a brief outline
of the older poetâs public image in the mid-to-late 1950s, to give an idea of the sort of figure that

Writing in the postscript to the 1976 reprint of Articulate Energy, Davie describes his first book of

collection, which comprised poems written between 1957 and 1963, declared that â[i]n these poems
Donald Davie abandons certain features of his writing which have attracted notice in his earlier volumes.
In particular he now eschews for the most part the use of irony, nostalgia, and literary allusions.â43

similar to the debased sociality to which Davie admits paying tribute through âinert gestures of social
adaptivenessââno doubtâ, âI supposeâ, âof courseâ, âalmostâ, âperhapsâ â.59 This is absolutely to be
distinguished from, for example, the metaphysical emphasis on âdiscretionâ in Prynneâs âA Gold Ring

| shopping-bagâ.60 When we encounter the âinert gesturesâ of âHave | you had enough? Do have a little

sarcasm, whereas in Davieâs poetry they retain their primary function as âpoetic socializingââwhat John
Press described in 1963 as âvaguely reassuring gesture[s] intended to show that these highly intellectual

In order to clarify Prynneâs poetic and academic relationship with Davie, it may help to compare

the gentle humour of the final sentence, that the undergraduate may speak on equal terms with the

The three events considered above show Davie introducing Prynne to the mechanisms of mainstream
poetic publicity and Prynne shifting between acquiescence and resistance. As early as September

Olson that âmy own Vol. of poems (out November) is 96% of no interest to me. Zip-fastener type of
thingâdead as no door-nail would be, given any decent chance.â91 It is instructive to compare this
with Tomlinsonâs early poetic career: after the publication of a pamphlet of juvenilia, Relations and
Contraries (1951), with Erica Marxâs Hand and Flower Press, it was Davieâs support of Tomlinsonâs
âfirst real collectionâ, The Necklace (1955), which helped him âfind [his] way into printâ in a more

felt ambivalent about using the cultural capital accumulated through Davieâs association with the
Movement in order to further their own very different poetics. From the late 1960s onwards, Prynneâs
aversion manifested itself in an almost total rejection of the mainstream literary world, the details of
which will be discussed in the first part of Chapter 2; Tomlinson, by contrast, had enjoyed the fruits of
Davieâs influence much earlier, and was thus able, once established, to distance himself less completely
from the older poet. Prynneâs early involvement with Davie nevertheless complicates any attempt to
present his work as some sort of sui generis avant-garde artefact, or even to characterise it primarily as
an English offshoot of the Olsonian poetic project.
Discussing a series of parodies of Movement poets written by Amis and Larkin in 1956, Zachary

figures, Amis was perfectly happy to send him the parodies for publication, with he and Larkin thereby
âincluding their detractors in a trick they hoped to play on their supporters.â93 Just as Amis and

to âpourâ their work into a conventional âliterary and economic mouldâ, as David Trotter writes of
Force of Circumstance.

iii. F.R. Leavis
Speaking at a colloquium on the history of Cambridge English in 2011, Prynne recalled some of the
well-known figures with whom he had become acquainted in what was then more than five decades of
almost continuous association with the Faculty: âI knew William Empson only slightly but I enjoyed
a free and easy relationship with Ivor Richards over many years, and we had many conversations. I
was even quite well acquainted with F.R. Leavis and his wife Queenie.â96 Prynne and F.R. Leavis are
very rarely mentioned in the same breathâyet when the apparently obvious political and aesthetic
differences between the two men are set aside, there is much to recommend the comparison. Both
came up to Cambridge from minor public schools after periods of military service; Prynne was elected
fellow of Caius relatively soon after his undergraduate degree while Leavis worked on a Ph.D., but
both served as teachers at the university for many decades; both maintained ambivalent relationships
with Cambridge as an institution, by turns benefiting from the privileges of relatively secure academic
positions and acting as âghosts in the machineâ, opposed to aspects of prevailing academic orthodoxy;
most importantly, both Leavis and Prynne have, to an extraordinary degree, inspired successive
generations of critical and poetic âfollowersâ, for want of a better term.97 Responding to a piece by
Robert Archambeau in 2010, Andrea Brady vigorously contests his use of the label âCambridge Schoolâ,

of Cambridge poets [. . . ]. A major anthology, Conductors of Chaos (Picador, 1996), ed.

After moving into university teaching positions, many of these poets have been involved, consciously
or otherwise, in the creation of a nascent academic infrastructureâcritical books, conferences,
Ph.D. supervisionânot totally dissimilar to that which grew up around the so-called âLeavisitesâ, though
smaller and largely situated at the tertiary rather than the secondary level of the British education
system. To take one of the more obvious examples, as of the 2013/14 academic year the Cambridge
English Faculty employed Ian Patterson, Rod Mengham, Simon Jarvis and Drew Milne. All four were
taught by Prynneâif not through direct supervision, then through lecturesâas undergraduates at
Cambridge in the 1960s, â70s and â80s. All four are poets whose work has been published by the same
small presses as Prynneâs (principally Equipage and Barque, the former being run by Mengham himself).
Most importantly, since completing doctoral degrees at Cambridge, all four have supervised further
undergraduate, masters or doctoral work on Prynne or related subjects.

On the basis of this brief biographical sketch, it seems fair to say that Prynne spent his sixth-form
and college years in an environment suffused by Leavisâs judgements and approach, if only as foils
for more catholic approaches to the canon, as in the first volume of Daichesâs Critical History of

The part of New Bearings from which Prynneâs argument can be seen most clearly to deriveâor at
least to draw inspirationâis the first chapter. Like Prynne, Leavis locates the fulcrum of his argument
at the point of transition from the Romantic to the Victorian, arguing that English poets of the later
nineteenth century failed to develop aesthetic techniques adequate to changes in the broader intellectual
climate and its language, clinging to outmoded âideas of the essentially poetical which, when the
conditions which gave rise to them [. . . ] changed, bar[red] the poet from his most valuable materialâ.110
At first glance, this emphasis on stasis might seem at odds with Prynneâs theory of an important change
between, say, Wordsworth and Tennyson, but these attitudes are really two sides of the same coin.
The change that Prynne identifies is a reconfiguration of the relationship between poetic language and
reality, not necessarily a change in poetic language itself: like passengers in a stationary train carriage,
Victorian poets think they are moving forwards when in fact they are simply watching an adjacent
train pull out of the station. Differentiating the meditative poetry with which he is concerned from
an earlier tradition, Prynne notes that âperformances such as âLycidasâ were essentially public events,
monuments crafted out of a shared language, in such a way that the feeling expressed was cogently
personal but also anonymousââthe problem for later poets is that the poetic language which they
continue to use is no longer âsharedâ in any real sense.111 Importantly, Prynne and Leavis not only

That Prynne considers himself at least partially exempt from this last comment is suggested by the

It is worth dwelling on what Prynne might actually mean by this phrase, and by his apparent
enthusiasm for the âcontingent eventâ in the passage quoted above. On one level, âforce of circumstanceâ
is a conventional phrase, usually preceded by âthroughâ or âbyâ and used to indicate unavoidable, external
pressures impinging on the subject of a sentence. The Times Digital Archive shows its first usage in the
newspaper in 1833, with much more regular appearances from the end of the Edwardian period. As a
title, âThe Force of Circumstanceâ had already been used by W. Somerset Maugham in 1928, while Steve
Clark notes that Prynneâs usage âperhaps alludes to Hardyâs Satires of Circumstanceâ.120 In the context
of this particular collection of poems, however, there seems to be a more pressing ideological-aesthetic
meaning. James Keery has claimed provocatively that âon the strength of this early collection alone
Prynne deserves to be recognised as the finest Movement poet bar Larkin and Davie.â121 Whether or
not this act of recuperation can be accepted in its entirety, Keeryâs earlier contention that âForce of
Circumstance is recognisably derivative from Davieâ is not substantially in doubt.122 Given this, it seems
appropriate to read the valorisation of âforce of circumstanceâ as an anti-Romantic, Movement-style
exhortation to modesty: an injunction not to interfere unduly with the forces that shape circumstance,
but simply to observe and to record them.123 This certainly seems to be force of the phrase in the
collectionâs titular poem, in which the speaker reflexively contemplates his or her own memory of a
âbroken, idle mill-wheelâ, repeatedly refusing to modify the image by making the wheel turn, or even by
imagining the reconstruction of âthe rotting chute | That brought the water to itâ in order to allow it
to turn of its own accord.124 For the speaker, âthe force | Of circumstance [. . . ] protects the placeâ,
preventing any such interference, though at the poemâs conclusion he or she is permitted to âset a heron
down, a tense | And watchful silenceâ which figures the speakerâs own position as observer.125

33
The phrase âcontingent eventâ, as used by Prynne, has the potential to confuse this interpretation.
The clearest source for this concept is Chapter 9 of Aristotleâs De Interpretatione, which raises the
famous question of the truth value of the proposition âA sea battle will take place.â If any proposition
must be either true or false, then whether or not the battle will take place must already have been
determined, a situation which apparently leaves no room for contingency. Citing the common-sense
perception of human will as causative, Aristotle attempts to reconcile these conflicting positions by
seeming to restrict the principle of bivalence: âEverything must either be or not be, whether in the
present or in the future, but it is not always possible to distinguish and state determinately which of
these alternatives must necessarily come about.â126 This ambiguous solution to what has been termed
the problem of future contingents provided the fuel for a long-running debate over the relationship
between human free will and Godâs omnipotence, particularly in the Scholastic period. Much of this
discussion was spurred by an influential commentary on Aristotleâs text by the late Roman philosopher
Boethius, an author to whom Prynne has acknowledged a profound debt.127 What, then, does Prynne
mean when he claims that âthe modern poet would hardly recognize a contingent event if he saw one,
and least of all if he had been expecting itâ? The traditional opposition between contingency and
necessity would seem to undercut his pointâsurely it is the necessary event which shows the true
force of circumstance, determined as it is by external forces rather than by human will? It is possible,
however, to suggest that in the context of âThe Elegiac Worldâ a necessary event would be one which fit
too neatly into the poemâs meditative structure, as conceived by the poet himself (pronoun Prynneâs).
A contingent event is in this sense the type of event most truly determined by circumstance. The final
part of Prynneâs commentââand least of all if he had been expecting itââwould seem on this reading
to be a joke at the expense of poets who are eager to bear witness to contingent reality, but only within
their own pre-conceived formal structures.

contemporary fiction, since too much of it is insipid, garrulous, and genteel to vanishing

writing when I see it, to find it there and reclaim it for those who will stop to read.135
Prynneâs description of what characterises âreal writingâ as a âserious ethical shapelinessâ is worth
considering in detail. What might at first glance seem like a string of weighty adjectives designed

Prynne about poetic form. In particular, the word âshapelinessâ echoes a 1962 paean to âthe poemâs
achieved shapeâ: âThis is not only the words as isolated monads, but their ordering; the grammar

conjunctions, maintained always by the constant verb.â136 This, in turn, recalls both the argument put
forward in the 1961 piece âResistance and Difficultyâ, which celebrates the ability of poems to become
real objects in the world, perceptible through their very resistance to perception, and the editorial
statement appended to Prynneâs issue of Prospect, which declares that â[w]ork sent in should preferably
be extensive enough to define its own contourâ.137 The most important five words of the quotation

Prynneâs position here is remarkable both for its elitism and for its pessimism: he seems at least to
acknowledge a structural component (âconspicuous abundanceâ) in the determination of the reading

iv. May Day and Metaphor

Exactly three months before the letter referred to above, Prynne sent Riley a postcard and a

A divergence between Prynne and Leavis can, nevertheless, be felt in their one clearly documented
encounter, which occurred in the letters pages of the Times Literary Supplement in 1971. On 6 May
that year, the TLS printed the text of a lecture titled âThe Osmotic Sapâ, delivered by the Movement
poet Roy Fuller in his role as Professor of Poetry at the University of Oxford. The theme of the lecture
was the interaction between poetry and science, one which might be expected to pique the interest both
of the recent author of Brass (1971) and of Leavis, who had famously engaged with C.P. Snow over
a similar issue during the âTwo Cultures debateâ less than a decade earlier. Fuller begins the lecture
with two extended quotations. The first is taken from Leavisâs celebrated ârevaluationâ of Shelley, first
printed in Scrutiny in 1935 and subsequently collected in Revaluation: Tradition and Development in
English Poetry (1936). Here, Leavis uses the second section of Shelleyâs âOde to the West Windâ to
launch a full-scale attack on the poetâs approach to metaphor: âWhat [. . . ] are those âtangled boughs
of Heaven and Oceanâ? They stand for nothing that Shelley could have pointed to in the scene before
him; the âboughs,â it is plain, have grown out of the âleavesâ in the previous line, and we are not
to ask what the tree is.â150 Making use of a biological metaphor himself, Leavis summarises what
he opposes in Shelley as âa general tendency of the images to forget the status of the metaphor or
simile that introduced them and to assume an autonomy and a right to propagate, so that we lose in
confused generations and perspectives the perception or thought that was the ostensible raison dâÃªtre
of imageryâ.151 Having quoted Leavis at length, Fuller proceeds to quote an extended passage from
the physicist and poet Desmond King-Heleâs Shelley: His Thought and Work (1960) in which every
detail of the âOde to the West Windâ is meticulously accounted for in modern meteorological terms.
The central thrust of the argument is that Shelleyâs âloose cloudsâ are of the fractostratus type, and
that â[t]he tangled boughs from which these leaf-like clouds are shaken are those regions of air whose
slightly adverse pressures, temperature and humidities make them the destined birthplace for clouds.â
Furthermore, â[t]hese parcels of air, turbulent, ever-changing in shape like wind-blown boughs, contain
a mixture of water-vapour from Ocean and air from Heaven.â152
Leavis was predictably unimpressed by the narrow scientism of this supposed refutation and wrote
to the editor of the TLS on 21 May to defend his 35-year-old argument:

defends the stanza, in order to reflect that imagery which has to be explained in this way

kind of felicity the meteorological specialist elucidates here. The reader who finds âshook
from the tangled boughs of Heaven and Oceanâ potent and right (as he may go on doing

the kind of actively intelligent attention Mr. King-Heleâs defence required of himâand

A final point of simultaneous comparison and difference can be found in Prynneâs and Leavisâs
respective attitudes to their mutual place of work, the University of Cambridge, and to the British
higher education system in general. Now considered a key figure in the history of Cambridge English,
Leavis spent much of his early career in positions with relatively low professional status, serving as a
probationary lecturer and supervising students at a number of different colleges.166 His biographer,
Ian MacKillop, summarises the precarity of Leavisâs position: âLeavisâs probationary lectureship ran
out in October 1931. Not until 1936 did he gain a lectureship again, and then only a part-time one.
He was kept on âpart workâ until 1947, when he was over fifty years old.â167 Leavis was not made a
fellow at Downing until 1936, at the age of 41, five years after starting work at the college.168 His most
important critical work, meanwhile, was not printed by Cambridge University Press until the 1960s,
appearing instead in the pages of Scrutiny, through Gordon Fraserâs appropriately named Minority
Press, or through metropolitan publishers such as Chatto & Windus. Despite this notable lack of
formal institutional support for the Leavisian project, Cambridge was nevertheless the community in
which that project took shape, and therefore came to mean something particular to its participants:
Perry Anderson describes Leavisâs ultimate conception of the city and its university as a sort of
âmeta-Cambridgeâ.169 In Scrutiny: A Retrospectâpublished, ironically, by CUP in 1963âLeavis makes
the ambivalence clear:

out, smash back to a more elemental predicament, since this is not in my nature. Or so I tell myself.
Merely to have deliberated in these terms would seem to me to have reduced the possibility to a level
of contrived irrelevance.â Importantly, Prynne presents his acceptance of the offer as an all-or-nothing
decision: âIâd not be capable of not doing [sic] what is required with less than complete care; I respect
the young too much, and know that they need their opportunities placed very accurately before them.â
This attitude reappears clearly in almost every letter in which Prynne mentions his teaching duties:
while he is often critical of what the English Tripos requires him to teach and is invariably exhausted
by the obligations of supervisions and marking, he is nevertheless fully committed to the intellectual
development of his students and obviously sees the university as at least a potential site for real literary
education.
A sense of Prynneâs broader attitude towards the university as an institution can be gleaned from
another set of letters to the Times Literary Supplement. In August 1963, Prynne wrote a letter to the
editor responding to a leader article that had appeared in the newspaperâs 9 August edition. Titled
âThe Libraryâs Placeâ, the article concerned the issue of public funding for university libraries, the
context being the rise of the new, so-called âplate glassâ universities and the imminent publication of
the Robbins Report, which was to give the official seal of approval to this unprecedented expansion of
the British higher education system. The author lamented the âgeneral parsimony of financial supportâ,
arguing for a co-operative acquisitions system comparable to the United Statesâ Farmington Plan,
which would allow newer and smaller libraries to build up strengths in specialist areas.172 For the

festivals, and so make all this a natural part of their young lives; and of yours also.183
The evident sincerity of this text makes using it to shore up a scholarly point about Prynneâs intellectual
influences seem churlish. Yet perhaps this feeling is itself symptomatic of a strong moral strain not only
in Prynneâs work but also in work on Prynne. This strain is certainly not pure Leavis, but it seems
clear that it owes at least something to the editor of Scrutiny.
v. Conclusion
This chapter has argued for the recognition of Leavis as an early influence on Prynne, based on the
content of prose works such as âThe Elegiac World in Victorian Poetryâ and on the style of Prynneâs
engagement with certain literary and political issues, most obviously in his correspondence. This
influence should not be surprising given that Leavisâs critical work and opinions enjoyed wide circulation

A clue may be found in two relatively unknown essays by Davie, published in the journal Twentieth

184 Defending Quintana against the apparently common view that his book is âearnest,
exhaustive, pedestrian, largely irrelevant but, for its minor virtue of accuracy about facts and dates,

Third Programme or in the weeklies, can be shown to voice opinions or to use methods
derived from Scrutiny.
190
At this stage, then, Davieâs position can be summarised as the advocacy of a long march through the
cultural institutions, grasping the spirit of Scrutiny in a non-dogmatic fashion and using it to bring
critical rigour to venues which Leavis himself, at least in the mid 1950s, would not have touched with
a barge pole. This attitude can be usefully compared to Prynneâs, considered at length above. As
has been shown in relation to Shelley, Prynne clearly abandoned certain Leavisian stricturesânotably
Leavisâs opinion of the limited role of metaphorâin his critical writing from the late 1960s onwards.
Yet if Davie is right to state that âDr Leavis has no methodâ and that attempts to claim otherwise are
the illegitimate protestations of âself-styled disciplesâ, then this shift need not be characterised as a
fundamental break. Rather, Prynneâs critical work can be seen to exist on a spectrum of Leavisian
influence, much as his attitude to the university shares elements of Leavisâs view while also differing in
several respects. Crucially, this mode of critical relation to Leavis, for all its apparent incompleteness,

Chapter 2. âThe inter-modifying force of open argumentâ
i. Introduction
Periodicals have played a crucial role in the distribution both of Prynneâs poetry and of his prose over
the course of the last five decades. Yet the category âperiodicalâ is a broad one, stretching to encompass
everything from photocopied âzinesâ to academic journals, from national newspapers to the annual
records of Oxford and Cambridge colleges. This chapter will focus on one specific form of periodical,
the little magazine, examining Prynneâs engagement with this formâand with the social and literary
milieu which attends itâthroughout his early career. It will then consider the interaction between
this publication context and Prynneâs prose, described in the Introduction to this thesis as the most
contextually transparent of his work, using the foregoing analysis to provide a detailed reading of the
1967 piece âA Note on Metalâ. Chapter 1 concluded that, by virtue of his rejection of the machinery of
mainstream poetic publicity in the mid-to-late 1960s, Prynne is âmore properly Leavisian than most
of the so-called Leavisitesâ; it will be the task of the present chapter to determine what this rejection
means in practice. As in the discussion of Davie and Leavis, special attention will be paid to periodicals
and points of contact which may have been overlooked in previous accounts, whether as a result of
the bias towards transatlantic influence that has already been described or simply due to the lack of
existing archival research on the very earliest stages of Prynneâs career. Before developing any sort of
argument, however, it will be necessary to provide a provisional definition of the little magazine.
Long a neglected area of literary studies, little magazines have experienced renewed academic
interest in recent years. The publication of David Miller and Richard Priceâs British Poetry Magazines
1914â2000: A History and Bibliography of âLittle Magazinesâ in 2006 coincided with the beginning of
Peter Brooker and Andrew Thackerâs Modernist Magazines Project, which has since resulted in an
online archive of scanned journals and the publication of three substantial reference volumes covering
magazines in Britain and Ireland, North America and continental Europe.193 These new resources
come on top of a number of existing bibliographical and critical works, many of which are listed in
Priceâs selected bibliography of little magazine scholarship.194 Nevertheless, there remain regions of
obscurity. One such region is occupied by little magazines published in Britain from the 1960s onwards,
which are excluded for reasons of chronology from Brooker and Thackerâs 2009 volume. Miller and
Priceâs largely bibliographical work on these periodicals supplements valuable oral history undertaken
193Modernist Magazines Project, <http://www.modernistmagazines.com/> [accessed 15 January 2015]; Peter Brooker
and Andrew Thacker, eds, The Oxford Critical and Cultural History of Modernist Magazines, Volume I: Britain and
Ireland 1880â1955 (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2009); Brooker and Thacker, eds, The Oxford Critical and Cultural
History of Modernist Magazines, Volume II: North America 1894â1960 (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2012); Brooker,
Thacker, Sascha Bru and Christian Weikop, eds, The Oxford Critical and Cultural History of Modernist Magazines,
Volume III: Europe 1880â1940 (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2013).
194Richard Price, âLittle Magazinesâ, British Library, <http://www.bl.uk/reshelp/findhelprestype/journals/
littlemagazines/littlemagazines.html> [accessed 15 January 2015].
51
by Wolfgang GÃ¶rtschacher, notably in his Little Magazine Profiles (1993).195 Nevertheless, that one of
the key resources in the field remains a Ph.D. project produced by an Austrian scholar more than two
decades ago is itself suggestive of the lack of critical attention to these magazines in Britain. Given the
dates of Prynneâs poetic activity, this is clearly problematic for any scholar of his work.
For the purposes of this chapter, the little magazine is understood to be a periodical concerned
primarily with literature (generally poetry), produced cheaply by volunteers formally independent of
any external institution and distributed for a short period of time to a small group of subscribers,
either for free or for a small fee.196 Production costs, pricing, length of operation and subscriber
numbers are all relative terms and it would be futile to impose any absolute upper limit on any of them.
Nevertheless, their consistent minimisation across the field of little magazine production points to a
single determinant, which can be identified as non-commerciality. Any little magazine which is sold for a
fee may, strictly speaking, be considered a commodity. Nevertheless, it is a relatively consistent feature
of little magazines that they strive constantly to escape the commodity form, either by being given
away for free or, if this is not financially viable, by minimising the proportion of surplus value returned
to their producers after sale (a proportion which, in any case, rarely exceeds the amount necessary to
produce the next issue of the magazine). Contributions to the running of a little magazine often take
the form of relatively unmediated provision of the materials for production and distribution, e.g. the
use of a mimeograph machine or a book of postage stamps. As a result of this dislocation from the
world of commercial publishing, little magazines of the type with which this chapter is concerned rarely
carry advertising, though there are exceptions to this rule, notably Prospect. These criteria constitute
a rough practical or economic definition of the little magazine. There is, however, a further, ideological
level to consider before the concept will come fully into focus. Little magazines have historically been
associated with modernismâspecifically, with the long canonical period of Euro-American modernism
extending roughly from the beginning of the First World War to the end of the Second. As such, the
attempt to define the little magazine in the 1960s and â70s encounters many of the problems inherent
in defining âlate modernismâ, a term which is frequently applied to Prynneâs workâincluding on the
dustjacket of his collected poemsâbut rarely in a critical or self-reflexive fashion.
The most detailed study of the term in specific relation to Prynne is provided by Mellorsâs Late
Modernist Poetics: From Pound to Prynne, a work whose subtitle suggests the broad outline of its
argument: that the âimpersonality, eclecticism, linguistic playfulness and Hermetic rigourâ of Prynneâs
poetry descends in a linear fashion from the work of Ezra Pound, via that of Charles Olson, and that
195Wolfgang GÃ¶rtschacher, Little Magazine Profiles: The Little Magazines in Great Britain, 1939â1993 (Salzburg:
University of Salzburg, 1993).
196The relationship between little magazine producers and external institutions is described as only âformally independentâ
in order to acknowledge the many forms of informal institutional support that exist. This is clearest in the case of
magazines produced by members of universities, who may enjoy privileged access to means of physical production such as
printers and photocopiersâor, in earlier periods, mimeograph machinesâin arrangements which are contingent on their
academic status and seniority.

the whole phenomenon can be described as late modernist in character.197 Yet Mellors also provides
an external, logistical definition of the category to complement this narrative of internal aesthetic
development. Introducing the section of the book which concerns Prynne, he notes that â[t]he real and
imaginary divisions between modernist and anti-modernist camps run very deep in British literary
culture and have only recently begun to seem archaicâ:
While in the final analysis the distinction between âmainstreamâ and âalternativeâ poetry is
bogus, the view taken by the institutions and prize-giving bodies that have evolved since
the 1950s has been one of pull-quote respect for the great modernists accompanied by
unwavering contempt for âforeignâ experiments carried out by British poets.198
It is this separationâan intellectually âbogusâ distinction which has taken on the character of reality
through the sheer fact of continued enforcementâwith which this chapter is partly concerned, accepting
the essential arbitrariness of the disjunction while investigating the real effects that it has exerted,
particularly on the work grouped under the âalternativeâ side of the equation.199 Mellors goes on to
link this situation directly to the non-commerciality of small presses and little magazines: âWhile
the small press might not be entirely free from the process of exchange, it nevertheless represents an
alternative to the market-led interests of established publishing houses.â200 Reading âlittle magazineâ for
âsmall pressâ, we can thus clarify the definition of the late modernist little magazine in Britain as one
which exists in a specifically antagonistic relationship to mainstream poetry publishing: not so much
non-commercial, then, as anti-commercial, rejecting exchangeâif not fully, then as far as possibleâas
part of a deliberate strategy of contestation. Crucially, Mellors presents Prynneâs involvement with
these magazines as equally deliberate, rather than simply as the result of his exclusion from other
channels: âWhere other poets hoped to work their way up from little magazines and presses to gain
greater recognition with Faber, Cape, Oxford University Press, and other major publishers, Prynne
reversed the process, placing higher value on small-scale enterprises.â201

reservation: âwhat he did see as less obviously helpful in the long run was the positive need for social
& cultural resistance, since while it functioned as a means of enhancing the concentration of effort
and serious practice, it also encouraged a defensiveness and stridency of tone which tended to shatter
the firm centre.â202 Having followed this train of thought, howeverââif the reaction to resistance (real

The clearest way to represent Prynneâs overall involvement with little magazines is visually. Figure
1 maps almost every periodical in which Prynneâs poetic work has been published, linking them to the
books in which the same work was subsequently collected.203 One of the principal uses of this mode of
visualisation is as a tool to investigate the periodisation of Prynneâs poetry, discussed at the beginning
of the previous chapter. For example, the severing of Force of Circumstance from Prynneâs later work
is apparently confirmed by the lack of connections between the five publications in which its poems
appearedâLyrische Hefte, Prospect, Satis, Mica and Tomorrowâand subsequent collections of the
1960s such as Kitchen Poems and The White Stones. On the other hand, the status of the supposed
second break between The White Stones and Brassâor, alternatively, somewhere in the middle of The
White Stonesâis altogether less clear: whatever its aesthetic ruptures, Prynneâs poetry from the late
1960s to the mid 1970s seems to be bound together by a relatively coherent web of periodical publication.
Meanwhile, the books proposed by Sutherland as representative of Prynneâs âlate styleâââfrom Red
D Gypsum, published in 1998, to the latest, Kazoo Dreamboats, from 2011ââare conspicuous by their
absence from the diagram, not having received magazine publication at all, suggesting a sort of negative
coherence in independence.204
Diagrams such as Figure 1 should not, however, be treated as absolute standards against which the
truth or falsity of various periodisation schemes can be measured. This is particularly the case when
it comes to an object of study as internally heterogeneous as Prynneâs career, which, as it is one of
the key aims of this thesis to emphasise, cannot simply be reduced to a series of chapbooks or to the
large yellow volume of the collected poems. When prose is considered alongside poetry, some of the
structures outlined in Figure 1 begin to break down: where, for instance, does âTomorrow is Fade Out
Nightâ fit, being a review of Douglas Woolfâs novel Fade Out (1959) printed in Prospect in 1964, well
after Prynne had repudiated his poetic style in Force of Circumstance but before the shift had been
confirmed by the publication of Kitchen Poems?

ii. Prynne and little magazines, 1955â1966
Prynneâs involvement with little magazines began as early as his time in the sixth form at St Dunstanâs
College, where in 1955 he was instrumental in the revival of a school publication, Six, which had last
appeared in 1948. Billed as âan unofficial production undertaken by members of the Sixth Formâ,
Sixâs 1955 issue was edited by Prynne, and it therefore seems fair to assume that its brief, unsigned
editorial statement was also written by him.207 This statement is conspicuous in its desire to place the
issue within a long-running tradition, or at least one that might seem long-running to a sixth-former.
According to the schoolâs archivist, the magazine had âappeared sporadically from the 1930sâ onwards,
a run which the teenage author describes as âalmost legendaryâ.208 He then proceeds to generalise about
previous editions of Sixâparticularly those produced during the Second World Warâclaiming that â[a]
generation stands mirrored in [their] pagesâ:
it may not perhaps be too rash to detect in comparison with [the present issueâs] forbears,
a paradoxically more balanced and less adult approach to life. The accelerated maturity
forced on the war generation has gradually relaxed its grip; and the recondite earnestness
and cynical obscurantism of the war-time SIXâs rings slightly strange to a generation which
has never been forced face to face with the bitter futility of war.209
The point of this comparison might seem to be to distance the 1955 cohort from their wartime
equivalents, men of Donald Davieâs generation who would by this point have been well on their way to
positions of adult authority, some perhaps serving as schoolmasters. Yet there is also, in the easy use
of the term âforbearsâ, a deliberate claim to continuityâindeed, the very act of making the comparison
implies that there is something comparable. The author strengthens the sense of tradition by going on
describe the schoolâs English master, Basil Harvey, as âthe spiritual god-father of SIX ever since its first
appearanceâ, before expressing hope that the revived magazine âwill not die an early death [. . . ] will
not sink back into oblivion.â210 The 1955 issue itself is a striking object. Large post quarto in format,

The magazines to which Prynne contributed as an undergraduate and postgraduate student are

212 Both founded in 1959, Prospect and Tomorrow are in many ways mirror images
of each other, with the axis of reflection situated somewhere along the newly-opened M1 motorway:
the former was edited by Elaine Feinstein, Tony Ward and eventually Prynne himself in Cambridge,
the latter by Ian Hamilton and Susil Pieris in Oxford.213 Both had reached their fourth issue by
the second half of 1960, each publishing a relatively broad range of more or less established poets,
though with almost no actual overlapâaside from Prynne, the only contributor shared by the two
magazines is Harold Pinter. This may testify to the relative insularity of the university communities
which both Tomorrow and Prospect represent, or at least the importance of personal contact in the
solicitation of work for publication, although both magazines also carried work by foreign poets, with
Prospect becoming known for championing the new American poetry represented by Olson, Dorn and
Robert Creeley, among others.214 If Prynneâs very earliest poetic publications remained confined to the

iii. The role of prose
The preceding section set out the diverse range of little magazines with which Prynne was involved in
the very earliest stages of his literary career. The present section will shift focus from the magazines
themselves to Prynneâs conception of the role of prose within them, examining a disjunction between his
intellectual position and his editorial practice. This gap will then be used to reflect on a specific prose
text, âA Note on Metalâ, in the following section, turning the focus back on to the intellectual context
of Prynneâs early career. Having so far been confined to the period leading up to the publication of the
first issue of the Intelligencer in 1966, the scope of the inquiry will now be extended to include this
particular periodical.
As the quotations from Prynneâs earliest letters to Olson and Dorn indicate, his attitude towards
prose as editor of Prospect was one of cynicism. His language when describing the magazine presents it
as a fortification which must be protected at any cost from the âhostile [and] parochial mediocrity [of
the] English universityâ, with âprose theoryâ being one of the key tools by means of which âthe wiseacresâ
might gain a âfootholdâ in its walls.247 If radical new American poetry is to be printed in England,
it must speak for itself. Indeed, if it is truly to speak at all, it can only do so by itself, unmediated
either by the mass-produced scholarship of the âknowledge industryâ or by professional criticismâwhat
Prynne describes to Dorn as âdiscussion/speculation about issues, poetic theory, problems of the
writerââboth of which are equally corrupt and decrepit.248 Yet there is one very obvious problem with
taking this anti-prose posture at face value: when Prospect 6 was published in Spring 1964, it included
prose. In fact, fully one third of the magazineâs 42 pages are devoted to it, in a broad array of forms
including fiction, review and âpoetic theoryâ, with Prynne himself contributing one of the key pieces
and Davieâa figure who, as Chapter 1 showed, was certainly not immune from charges of wiseacrey by
246Tim Carmody, âPaper Modernismâ, Snarkmarket (9 August 2009), <http://snarkmarket.com/blog/snarkives/
selfdisclosure/paper_modernism/> [accessed 29 January 2015].
247Prynne to Olson, 26 November 1961.
248Prynne to Dorn, 17 June 1965; Prynne to Dorn, 29 November 1961.

this pointâcontributing another. The potential explanations for this contradiction are twofold: either
Prynneâs original editorial vision for Prospect 6 was compromised by social or economic forces outside

âpressures inherited that I canât side-stepâ mean that Prospect will have to carry a review of Davieâs

lends weight to the latter explanation. While the inclusion of Davieâs contributionâa short piece on
the American poet Alan Stephensâmight perhaps be attributed to âpressures inheritedâ (though it

publication for three years and eventually bankrupting the magazineâcould have tolerated such an

That Prynneâs conception of the relationship between prose and little magazines evolved away from
the exclusionary rhetoric of 1961 is confirmed by one particularly revealing letter sent to Peter Riley
six years later. The letter apparently accompanied a copy of the so-called âprose issueâ of The English
Intelligencer, which though edited by Riley for its second series was substantially producedâin the
material sense of physical duplication, and often in terms of arranging the content into stencilsâby
Prynne, with the assistance of both Andrew and Jean Crozier and John James.251 As Latter notes,
the worksheet had under Rileyâs editorship been moving steadily away from being simply a collection
of poems and towards the provision of a more comprehensive range of âinformationâ, generally in the
form of long letters and other prose pieces. Interviewed by Latter for a doctoral thesis, James recalls
that âthe balance between poetry and non-poetry was increasingly skewed. Towards the end Jeremy

enough was enough. [. . . ] This was the moment of what I refer to . . . as the Palace Revolution, the
night of 6 December 1967.â252 By âPalace Revolutionâ, James is referring to the removal of Riley from
the editorship of the worksheet by Prynne, Crozier and himself.253

The letter from Prynne to Riley under consideration here can be situated precisely in the period
referred to by James as âtowards the endâ, being sent in mid September 1967. Rileyâs intention to print
a formal âprose issueâ had been announced at least twice in previous issues of the Intelligencer; yet
once it had been put together in its final form, Prynne described the collection which he had just sent
off to Riley as âa weak assemblage, mostly midget fictions or would-be lyric gesturesâ.254 It is clear
that Prynneâs unhappiness with the issue stems not from the bare fact of printing prose rather than
poetry, but from what he sees as the poor quality of the prose that Riley has chosen to print (though he
may have been choosing from a limited pool): âif a whole culture supports itself on its prose (whatever
it might aspire onwards from that base), is this all we have?â255 Jamesâs collocation of the âskewedâ
balance between âpoetry and non-poetryâ with âJeremy express[ing] his dissatisfactionâ is therefore
misleading; in fact, Prynne seems to assign a hugely important role to prose, describing it as nothing
less than the âbaseâ of culture. He continues in this vein:
We canât & I presume shouldnât presume expect to evolve all our thinking into poems,
where the complication of opinion and the inter-modifying force of open argument has to
give way to the closed form. A poem isnât âto be disagreed withâ and thus evolutionary &
self-extending: it has a discrete priviledge as self-sufficient (âfor its own sakeâ, whatever
that means), just as soon as itâs set down on paper. I would look to prose to expand the
base, to equip and exercise the person, literally to keep us sane. Otherwise we topple into
bigotryâour poems, though of course not considered, not level & judged, must be right.
Prose is the way such extension into the world might be less armour-plated, more provisional
for heavenâs sake, and I donât mean picturesque short stories.
According to this statement, prose not only is the base of culture, but also serves to expand that base
according to an âevolutionaryâ logic: argument and disagreement are implicitly procreative, generating
new members of the community of ideas. Prose is a form defined by its provisionalityâits competence, if
not always willingness, âto be disagreed withââand therefore forms a natural home for âthe complication
of opinion and the inter-modifying force of open argumentâ. This phenomenon, in turn, through a process
which Prynne describes as âexerciseâ, âkeep[s] us saneâ and prevents âusâ from âtoppling into bigotryâ.
Though expressed in sweeping terms, this is hardly a controversial view of the role of argumentative
discourse. In fact, it could be read as a condensation or a restricted prefiguration of any number of
eminently uncontroversialâeven liberalâmodels of discursive rationality, from the Socratic method
to JÃ¼rgen Habermasâs Theory of Communicative Action (1981). By the same token, it contains some
common blind-spots, most notably the obscurity of the referent of the collective noun âusâ, a group
which is able to assume sanity and freedom from bigotry but which is never formally defined, and which
is by implication fringed by a penumbra of insane bigots.

iv. âA Note on Metalâ
âA Note on Metalâ is the most widely distributed of Prynneâs prose works prior to texts such as 2008âs
âHutsâ and 2010âs âMental Ears and Poetic Workâ, which owe their circulation to the relatively recent
phenomena of the online journal and the easily shareable PDF file. The work was first published in
The English Intelligencer as one of a series of responses to Peter Rileyâs âWorking Notes on British
Prehistory, or Archaeological Guesswork Oneâ, which had appeared as a supplement at the conclusion
of the worksheetâs first series.268 It was subsequently printed in pamphlet form alongside the poems
âAristeas, in Seven Yearsâ, âStar Damage at Homeâ and âFrost and Snow, Fallingâ, and has since been
included in every edition of Prynneâs collected poems.269 âA Note on Metalâ is, then, a text with more
than one context: initially embedded in the argumentative back-and-forth of the Intelligencer described
earlier in this chapter, it has also been presented as supplementary to a series of poems and as a
relatively self-sufficient, âstandaloneâ piece.270 It is the first and last of these contexts with which this
discussion is primarily concerned. As noted in the Introduction to this thesis, both C.D. Blanton and

Seeta Chaganti have examined âA Note on Metalâ in detail, while Simon Jarvis, Robin Purves, Reitha

exception, these accounts use the text as tool to examine some other aesthetic objectâusually one of
Prynneâs poems, or, in the cases of Blanton, Chaganti and Rider, poems by Basil Bunting and Geoffrey
Hill, Anglo-Saxon artefacts and twentieth-century sculpture respectively. Blantonâs account of the piece
is by far the most detailed, situating it in a political tradition occupied simultaneously by Ruskin and
Marx. Nevertheless, this reading is offered with the ultimate intention of making a point about poetic
practice and form, rather than of considering the text on its own terms, as prose. Rather than cite a
pre-existing summary of âA Note on Metalâ and risk distorting its analysis in the direction of another
scholarâs project, this section will therefore begin with a fresh paragraph-by-paragraph synopsis, before
undertaking a political reading of the text which will engage with existing judgements, particularly
Blantonâs, where appropriate. The synopsis will take the more opaque aspects of the text at face value,
leaving their explanation to the subsequent analysis. For the sake of clarity, however, a rough schematic
representation of the pieceâs argument has been provided in Figure 2.
Â¶1 (p. 128272): Before the early Bronze Age, property resided uncomplicatedly in substance, and
the âmost specific carrier for the inherence of powerâ was weight, found most obviously in stone. Stone
is ambiguous, offering both a hard outside and, through erosion or carving, a protective, sheltering
inside, but weight is its crucial property. Â¶2 (128): Metals such as copper and tin are more complex,
having âbrightness, hardness [and] ductilityâ as well as weight. Alloys abstract property from substance
even further, making it formal. The naturally uneven geographical distribution of metallic elements
encouraged the Sumerians to acquire tin from as far away as Cornwall; yet there is an obvious difference
in intent between this sort of trade and the physical transportation of the materials for Stonehenge.
Â¶3 (128): The strength of metal when forged in fire was âinfinitely more abstractâ than that of flint or
wood and allowed for rapid developments in hunting, fishing and agriculture. A âspiritualâ importance
thus became attached to the sorts of sharp edges characteristic of metal tools, marking a shift from
âthe history of substance (stone)â into âthe theory of power (metal)â. Â¶4 (128â29): That said, this
shift may actually have caused a âdeeper cultural adherenceâ to stone, a material not primarily related
to the practicalities of everyday active life, but to a different sort of âpersistence through material
transmutationââhence its use to mark death. Â¶5 (129): Advances in metallurgy shifted communities
more rapidly from hunting to agriculture, allowing for the idea of âplaceâ and making mining possible.
Threshing of grains encouraged the development of a theory of value even more abstract than the
âmixed relativism of substanceâ associated with alloys: a âspecialised function [. . . ] dependent on the
rate of exchange.â General abstraction of value through metallurgy and otherwise became the basis

presenceâ. Because this sort of currency is best suited to trading over large distances, trade routes
expanded. Â¶7 (130): The shift of wealth from power to value can be seen most clearly in interactions
between different societies, e.g. âthe sea-borne traders of the Eastern Mediterranean and the peoples of

of this flight, but also of âan exilic (left-wing) history of substance.â Â¶9 (130â31): This is a complex
and involuted history. Abstraction of value is not always deleterious and unmediated substance is not
always to be favoured, and is perhaps not even possible at all. Human awareness is sharpened by some
of the âshiftsâ away from substance, and stone is, in fact, already abstraction (âof standing, of balanceâ).
It is not a matter of identifying the crucial and catastrophic turning point, but of looking clearly at
âthe increasing speed of displacement which culminates only later in the critical overbalance of intent.â
As will by now be obvious, Prynneâs style in âA Note on Metalâ is highly telegraphic. The text
contains many gapsânot so much gaps in archaeological evidence, which is amply provided in the series
of dense endnotes, but gaps in argument. Frequently, Prynne will move from the specific to the general
in a transition so sharply telescopic that it cannot but be disorienting, even for experienced readers
of his work. At other times, he will make use of apparently familiar words or phrases in radically
unfamiliar contexts, rendering already convoluted sentences even more difficult to parse. Ryan Dobran
claims that âA Note on Metalâ is a text which âfails to explicate or consistently group the various
concepts it discusses; it is patchy and meandering.â273 On the contrary, the textâs difficulty stems
precisely from its tightly compressed structure, a structure which admits gaps (it is âpatchyâ), but
which is far from âmeanderingââin fact, it is highly organised. This discussion will now address five key
lacunae in âA Note on Metalâ, with the hope both of further clarifying Prynneâs text and of approaching
the relationship between prose, politics and history which is to be the ultimate subject of this section.
The choice of which gaps to analyse is necessarily influenced by the contours of the synopsis given
above.
1. Prynne relies on a number of technical philosophical termsâmost obviously âsubstanceâ, but
also âabstractionâ and âqualityââwithout explaining the context in which they are used, or making any
273Dobran, âJ.H. Prynneâs âAristeas, in Seven Yearsâ and The English Intelligencerâ, Journal of British and Irish
Innovative Poetry, 5.2 (2013), 20â42 (p. 39, n. 15).

reference to the intellectual tradition from which they are taken.
This is perhaps the most serious issue to be confronted in any reading of âA Note on Metalâ, with
the interpretation of Prynneâs basic terminology likely to inform almost every aspect of subsequent
analysis. Foremost among the terms used by Prynne is âsubstanceâ, a concept with a history almost
as long as philosophy itself. In the present context, and given the established contours of Prynneâs
philosophical preoccupations, its most relevant formulation would appear to be that which derives
from Hegelâs reading of Spinoza. Hegel famously claimed, in his 1805â06 lectures on the history of
philosophy, that âthought must begin by placing itself at the standpoint of Spinozism; to be a follower
of Spinoza is the essential commencement of all Philosophy.â274 What made Spinoza important for
Hegel was the formerâs repudiation of Cartesian dualism, which resulted in a form of substance monism,
substance being identified with a pantheistic God. For Spinoza, in Hegelâs reading, â[t]he true is simply
and solely the one substance, whose attributes are thought and extension or nature: and only this
absolute unity is reality, it alone is God.â275 This dissolving of the Cartesian subjectâobject divide
makes room for Hegelâs more complex reinterpretation of the subject as object, with his emphasis on
dialectically achieved self-consciousness. Prynneâs argument in âA Note on Metalâ, however, seems to
differ significantly from the Hegelian perspective. Most obtrusive in this respect is his apparent lack of
concern for subjective self-consciousness or will; or, to reframe it positively, his persistent concern with
the object. Throughout âA Note on Metalâ, the direction of argument extends relentlessly from material
conditions to human action. When the latter is described, it is invariably as a consequence of the
former, which goes on in turn to create further material conditions and further consequences. Croesus
may have created a bimetallic currency system (Â¶8), but this was the inevitable result of the availability
of the relevant materials, and he certainly didnât reflect on his role in the ever-increasing abstraction of
value (or, if he did, this fact is irrelevant). In other words, Prynneâlike Hegelâs most famous reader,
Marxâis rigorously materialist in his approach.276 As Sutherland puts it, ventriloquising Prynne, âthe
subject who engrosses the whole of truth is a disgusting contraption for silencing objects [. . . ] and is
fundamentally predatory and imperial in its reachâ.277
Why, then, does Prynne use the philosophically loaded term âsubstanceâ, when there are plenty of
other wordsââmatterâ, for exampleâwhich would allow him to avoid idealist associations altogether?
Why, indeed, does he not reframe his account in such a way that it would be unnecessary to name that
which is abstracted from, given that any technical termââsubstanceâ, âmatterâ, âbeingââis inevitably
already an abstraction? âA Note on Metalâ is, after all, a text which is explicitly concerned with the
deleterious effects of abstraction, making the reproduction of that process in its own form particularly
274G.W.F. Hegel, Lectures on the History of Philosophy: Medieval and Modern Philosophy, trans. by E.S. Haldane and
Frances H. Simson (Lincoln, NE: University of Nebraska Press, 1995), p. 257.
275Ibid., p. 256.
276In fact, as will be argued later in this chapter, Prynne isâdeliberatelyâeven more of a materialist than Marx.
277Sutherland, âSub Songs versus the subject: Critical variations on a distinction between Prynne and Hegelâ, in Hix
