glaring. It is possible to offer a preliminary explanation by relating Prynneâs use of language to his
concern for the object described above. For Prynne, as the early text âResistance and Difficultyâ makes
clear, language once used takes on an ontological status comparable to that of any other object in
the world, contributing to a general âresistanceâ which is experienced as âdifficultyâ and which gives
us a form of immediate knowledge about our surroundings.278 This position leads to an apparently
paradoxical situation in which, as Sutherland puts it, âthe truth of language [is] conceived to exist
âprior toâ any subjectâ, if not in an ontological sense than at least in a cognitive one.279 At the point of
composition, then, Prynne is faced with a pre-existing philosophical vocabulary which is the product,
over millennia, of the very process of abstraction which âA Note on Metalâ seeks to chart: âA Note on
Metalâ is, in other words, a text which attempts to describe the conditions of possibility of its own
terminology. The word âsubstanceââas with every other word used in the textâmay therefore be seen
not only as a tool with which to grasp an object, but also as an object in itself. From this perspective,
Prynneâs choice of this particular term signifies something concrete about the anti-idealist trajectory
of his argument. The consequences of this position will be discussed in more detail at the end of this
section in relation to the work of the philosopher Quentin Meillassoux.
2. In Â¶2, Prynne states that â[t]he differences between the kinds of intent supporting the movement
of bluestones from Pembrokeshire to Wessex, and the Sumerian acquisition of tin from Cornwall or
Bohemia, must be obvious.â It is not immediately clear why they must.
Since the publication of âA Note on Metalâ, a number of important archaeological discoveries have
been made concerning both Bronze Age cassiterite (tin ore) mining in Asia and the origins of the
Stonehenge bluestones, casting doubt on the models accepted by Prynne. Most notably, evidence has
emerged of mining in what are now western Afghanistan and southern Turkey, potentially stronger
candidates for the source of Sumerian tin than either the Czech Republic or Britain, while modern
chemical analysis has strengthened an existing theory that the bluestones were transported from Wales
to Wiltshire by glacial action rather than by deliberate human effort.280 These discoveries do not,
however, affect the fundamentals of Prynneâs comparison, and may in fact help to clarify it. Prynne is
not primarily concerned with the particular geographical regions from which materials were sourced,
or even with the distances involved in transporting them from source to destination, but rather with
âthe kinds of intent supporting the movementâ (Â¶2). In the case of Stonehenge, the intent, whether
astronomical or ritualâand whether the stones were transported all the way from Wales or from a more
proximate locationâis singular: the stones were sourced, moved and arranged for a specific reason,

3. Prynneâs central concern in Â¶3 with the shift from âthe history of substance (stone)â to âthe

The point here is similar to that which was at stake in the previous paragraph. Having described
the various uses to which forged metal might be putâploughshares, arrowheads, fish hooksâPrynne
asserts that â[t]he new quality of spiritual transfer was concentrated in these most durable forms of

his theory of a general shift from substance to power. Part of the obscurity of this statement derives
from the specialist sense of the word âmotiveâ, which is usedâas it is by V. Gordon Childe, whose
work provides one of the key spurs for âA Note on Metalââas a synonym for or variant spelling of
âmotifâ (see OED definition 7281). Even more confusing, however, is the phrase âquality of spiritual
transferâ: this is the first appearance of the word âspiritâ in the text, and, as with âtransferâ, it is not
at all clear in what sense it is being used. A reading of the following paragraph helps to illuminate
this passage. Here, Prynne shifts the focus of the piece back to stone, and refers to certain activities
specifically as âextension[s] of the bodyâ (Â¶4). Importantly, he implies the existence of a number of
different types of extension whose characteristics are determined by the directions in which they point
relative to the subjectâinward (as âego-termsâ) or outward (towards âenemies or animals hunted for
foodâ [Â¶4], etc.)âand which reflect these characteristics back on to the materials that they employ.
âSpiritual transferâ can, then, be seen as another term for bodily extension, also described as âpersistence
through material transmutationâ (Â¶4). When used in relation to metal, it serves to emphasise the
phenomenological sense in which subjectivity is always bound up with its objects: having developed
tools of unprecedented power and effectiveness, humans in some sense become those tools, as well as the
abstracting processes through which they are produced.282 â[D]urable forms of leading edgeâ (Â¶3) not
only represent the pinnacle of then-current technological development but also, having been invested

4. In Â¶5, Prynne claims that â[t]he threshing of millet or barley must bring a âpurerâ and more
abstract theory of value; the mixed relativism of substance leads, by varied but in outline predictable
stages, to value as a specialised function and hence as dependent on the rate of exchange.â Again, it is
not clear that the various parts of this statement follow necessarily from each other.
This sentence is formed of two parts: a blunt, demonstrative clause followed, after the semicolon,
by a more closely argumentative statement purporting to explain what goes before it. Obscurity is
fostered by the use of logical markers such as âmustâ and âhenceâ in close proximity to highly technical
phrases such as âthe mixed relativism of substanceâ, the meaning of which is not clearly explained. In
order to clarify Prynneâs argument, it is necessary to look closely at what distinguishes the activity
of threshing from other pursuits, principally hunting and fishing, that have already been described.
The reference to threshing follows a description of how systematic agricultureâfacilitated by new
metal toolsââproduces the idea of place as the chief local factâ (Â¶5), situating communities in fixed
geographical spots and making long-term local activities such as mining practicable. Threshing, by
which cereal grains are separated from their chaff, is even more specifically local than agriculture in
general, requiring a specially flattened and hardened threshing floor. Crucially, it is also extremely
labour-intensive, âabsorb[ing] as much as a quarter of agricultural labor inputâ prior to mechanisation
in the nineteenth century.284 This combination of factors creates a relatively novel situation in which
humans spend large amounts of time creating value (in the form of edible grains) through a more or
less stationary, repetitive activity. This is at the heart of Prynneâs argument about threshing: that
the relative complexity of the process and its insertion in a long chain of other processesâsowing,
harvesting, milling, etc.âdirectly encourages the development of âa more abstract theory of valueâ (Â¶5),
perhaps even an implicit labour theory of value. By âthe mixed relativism of substanceâ (Â¶5), then, he is
referring to the fungibility, or at least comparability, of properties such as hardness (metal) and edibility
(grain) that emerges from complex processes such as alloying and flour production, while âvalue as a

5. Prynne appears to suggest in Â¶6 that trade routes expanded as a function of the development of
numerable metal currency. This view is potentially controversial both for factual reasons (as a result
of its emphasis on the role of early coinage in foreign trade) and for what it seems to imply about
technological development and human agency.
Having described the development of techniques of casting and stamping bronze, which result in
the production of coins standardised by weight and design, Prynne offers the following observation:
âIn consequence, trade-routes undergo a dramatic expansion, since mercantile theory operates with
the most conspicuous success when employed over large inter-cultural distancesâ (Â¶6). He then quotes
a passage from Childe: âIn the Early Bronze Age peninsular Italy, Central Europe, the West Baltic
Coastlands, and the British Isles were united by a single system for the distribution of metalware, rooted
in the Aegean market.â285 Confusingly, this quote and the statement of Prynneâs that precedes it bear
no obvious relation to each other. Using the phrase âEarly Bronze Ageâ, Childe is referring to a period
unambiguously prior to the development of stamped coinage described by Prynne, which did not emerge
until c. 700 BC at the earliest. Further obscurity is created by the citation at the end of the previous
paragraph of an article by C.M. Kraay which seems directly to contradict Prynneâs argument.286
As Prynne summarises Kraayâs thesis, which is supported by an analysis of the distribution of coins
between numerous extant hoards, â[e]ven in the case of early Greek coinage [. . . ] the original intention
was not so much in the first place to facilitate trade (external or internal), but rather to establish a
system of centralised national wealth which could support a professional military casteâ (n. 3). This
knot cannot be untied as easily as those previously discussed, unless Prynneâs emphasis in the pieceâs
final paragraph on indeterminacyââ[i]f we are confident over the more developed consequences, at the
unrecognised turn we are still at a loss to say where or whyâ (Â¶9)âcan be taken as a quite serious
admission of ignorance and a declaration of openness to multiple explanations, of which Kraayâs is one.
Even then, it would seem strange to provide practically no evidence for oneâs own theory while shoring
up a competing narrative in the endnotes.
Ultimately, as with much else in this text, the statement that follows Prynneâs confident â[i]n
consequenceâ must be taken on trust. Even then, however, the teleology of the argument requires
further analysis. Prynne is not claiming here that coinage was developed in order to facilitate trade;
on the contrary, his theory is that âinter-culturalâ trade boomed as a result of the development of
coinage and âmercantile theoryâ (Â¶6). This is an important aspect of the argument in âA Note on Metalâ,
recalling the earlier emphasis on materials as vessels for âspiritual transferâ; for Prynne, metal is not
merely a passive object shaped by a pre-existing human will, but rather the motor and determinant of

6. At the end of Â¶8, Prynne associates âthe technique of [the] removalâ from âpresence as weightâ
with the emergence both of âa complete middle classâ and of âan exilic (left-wing) history of substanceâ.
It is not obvious what he means by either of the latter two terms, or how they relate to each other.
By this stage of the piece, it is relatively clear what Prynne means by âthe remov[al]â from âpresence
as weightâ (Â¶8)âthis is the tendency that has been described throughout âA Note on Metalâ, according
to which technological developments in metallurgy encourage value to be constituted in a series of
increasingly abstract forms, culminating (for the time being) in bimetallic systems of stamped metal
coins. Yet what follows this is more obscure: Prynne seems to set up a new, explicitly political
distinction between a âmiddle classâ and an âexilic (left-wing) historyâ, describing the two as being in
âcontrastâ (Â¶8). Perhaps the most important stumbling block here is historical: having just described
developments in currency during the reign of Croesus, Prynneâs use of the terms âmiddle classâ and
âleft-wingâ is apt to seem highly anachronistic, even if their emergence is qualified as merely âpossibleâ
(Â¶8)âthis is, indeed, a central issue with âA Note on Metalâ, which will be discussed in more detail
below. The most plausible definition of âmiddle classâ in this context is a relatively strict economic
one: Prynne is obviously not referring, in the modern British sense, to an organic combination of
professionals and other white-collar workers with elements of the labour aristocracy, but rather to a
class similar to that which Marx describes as manipulating âmerchantâs capitalâ in historical eras prior
to capitalismâs dominance of the mode of production.288
As for âleft-wingâ, the termâs immediate contextââan exilic (left-wing) history of substanceââ
suggests that it refers to a narrative about the past produced in the present, rather than to the essence
of past social structures or other forms of life: a phenomenon need not be left-wing itself for it to be
included in a left-wing history. Nevertheless, there is a further implication contained in the apparent
anachronism of both terms. By inserting these essentially social terms into a profoundly non-social,
material account, Prynne seems to make a claim to be more materialist than any existing brand of
political analysis, notably Marxism. The history of all hitherto existing society may be the history
of class struggle, but the history of all class struggle is the history of stone and metal. Crucially,
Prynneâs history is âexilicâ, and thus presumably to be differentiated from the long process of collective
solidarity and struggle that more commonly goes under the name âleft-wingâ. In fact, the term âexilicâ
specifically recalls his contemporaneous quotation to Riley of Shelleyâs description of the âgreat sandy
287This is perhaps the inverse (or the equivalent from a reversed perspective) of James Lovelockâs view of humanity
as nothing more than an element in a chemical reaction: âOur mistake when we started the Anthropocene was to fail
to notice that inadvertently we were the catalyst of the reaction between the carbon of coal and the oxygen of the airâ
(James Lovelock, A Rough Ride to the Future [London: Allen Lane, 2014], p. 10).

desert of politicsâ, across which Prynne suggests they should journey as âhonourable Beduinsâ.289 The
real question, then, consists of two parts: (a) what does Prynne understand an âexilic (left-wing) history
of substanceâ actually to be? and, (b) is âA Note on Metalâ an attempt to write, or at least to begin to
write, this history?
Blanton provides a lucid response to the first part of this question in the course of a summary of
Prynneâs argument:
With the advent of metal, significance no longer resides strictly in objects, and substance
accordingly begins to forget its own terrestrial origins. [. . . ] The qualitative hegemony of
metal, however, does not fully eclipse the substantiality of stone, but rather complicates and
even sublates it. Indeed the power of economy relies on its capacity to preserve substance
as a sedimented concept or ground, as a sort of historical unconscious against or across
which power implicitly measures itself. [. . . ] The attempt to remember a writing older than
monetary circulation or the commodity [. . . ] offers an Archimedean critical point outside of
economy, a position from which to induce, perform, or merely conceive modernityâs end.290
Following John-Joseph Goux, Blanton argues that âthe gesture to an origin of economics or an origin
of capital fatally undermines the hegemonic notion of the symbolââa left-wing history of substance
consists precisely in the performance of this gesture.291 But is this performance actually undertaken by
Prynne in âA Note on Metalâ, or does he merely gesture towards a gesture? On this point, Blanton is
equivocal, describing âpoetryââparticularly Prynneâsâas âan index to the history of substanceâ, but
neglecting to state explicitly whether or not âA Note on Metalâ is a member of this category.292 It is
certainly true, and was pointed out at the beginning of this discussion, that the text is included in a
book titled Poems, a status afforded to none of Prynneâs other prose works except the fictional âPlant
Time Manifold Transcriptsâ which follow Wound Response. On the other hand, it would be premature
to assume, as Chaganti seems to, that the simple fact of being published in this book makes âA Note
on Metalâ a âprose poemââtaken to its logical extreme, this line of reasoning would assign a similar
status to the authorâs note or the index of first lines.293
This dilemma can be avoided entirely by denying, or at least modifying, the premise of Blantonâs
argument: that a left-wing history of substance is to be found only in poetry. In fact, âA Note on Metalâ
does not contain a single reference to poetry. Blantonâs claim appears to rest on the fact that Prynne
is, by common consent, a poet, and that he must therefore be committed to this form above all else.
Yet it might be countered that he is many other things, among them a teacher and a writer of prose. It
is true that âA Note on Metalâ was first published in a context in which most of the prose was focused,
explicitly or otherwise, on the question of what sort of poetry English poets should be producing in the
mid-to-late 1960sâcertainly this was the implicit focus of Rileyâs âWorking Notes on British Prehistoryâ,
289Prynne to Riley, 15 September 1967.
290Blanton, p. 130.
291Ibid.

293Chaganti, p. 48.

to which Prynne was, in part, responding.294 Nor is it necessary to deny that Prynneâs conception of

âA Note on Metalâ accords fundamental importance to history, both as a word and as a concept.
The text is, self-evidently, a work of history itself, though this may not be its only function. It also
seeks a history, the âexilic (left-wing) history of substanceâ referred to above. Importantly, Prynne
deliberately counterposes the concepts of history and theory, most obviously at the end of the third
paragraph, where âthe history of substance (stone) shifts with complex social implication into the
theory of power (metal)â (Â¶3), but also in the reference to the â âpurerâ and more abstract theory of
valueâ (Â¶5) brought into being by threshing. A theory is, in a basic sense, the forward- or present-facing
counterpart of a history: a descriptive structure which attempts to account for a phenomenon while
remaining open to potential falsification. For Prynne it seems to be coded negatively, signifying the
increasing abstraction which it is the main purpose of âA Note on Metalâ to chart, a position which
would restrict the text to something like the simple backwards/forwards schema put forward by Chris
Nealon in a recent consideration of Prynneâs career.295 But does history need to be backwards-facing?
On one level, it obviously does; yet âa historyâ, no matter how exilic, is also something which underlies
and moves at the same speed as the present. In an ideal world, history and theory meet and mutually
inform each other in the present; in âA Note on Metalâ, however, this correspondence is ruptured by
theories of power and value which have deliberately forgotten their past. So, to return in a modified
form to a question asked above: is the text an attempt to excavate this history?
âA Note on Metalâ seems to be a provisional attempt to excavate an âexilic (left-wing) history of
substanceâ, but one which acknowledges that the task of full excavation is out of reach of any one text.
The clearest point of acknowledgement can be found in the final paragraph, in which Prynne appears
to retractâor at least to qualifyâmuch of the foregoing argument. Having set out an eminently linear
historical narrative, he now declares that this is not enough: a real history would not be âa simple
decision that any one movement is towards ruinâ (Â¶9). Importantly, however, Prynne does not take
this opportunity to declare the impossibility of such a history. While he acknowledges that âat the
unrecognised turn we are still at a loss to say where or whyâ (Â¶9), he refuses to hypostatise this loss as
some sort of fundamental characteristic of modernity or of the human condition in general. That an

âexilic (left-wing) history of substanceâ is hinted at but not fully investigated by âA Note on Metalâ can
be seen even more clearly by assessing the text against Prynneâs own criteria for prose, as communicated
to Riley shortly after the pieceâs first publication in the Intelligencer.
Discussing the passage from Shelley referred to earlier in the context of an argument about the role
of prose, Prynne claims that âthe pathos of Shelleyâs tract, noble as it is, urgently has to be re-written
for us now, with all the hormonal sophistication of birth-control & modern demography.â The emphasis
here is on continuity: not a crude âupdatingâ of A Philosophical View of Reform, but an effort to break
out of short-term political thinking through the identification of parallels between 1820 and 1967, with
the ultimate aim of laying bare deeper historical forces. Yet this sort of âre-writingâ is something which
âA Note on Metalâ deliberately forgoes. As mentioned above, the pieceâs context implies relevance to a
community of poets in late-1960s England, but the textâs actual history stops in the ancient world.
Prynne spends eight paragraphs describing several thousand years of developments in materials from
the Stone Age to the Iron Age, but offers only the most brief and suggestive account of how these
developments relate, for example, to currency as it exists in the mid-to-late twentieth century, and
no account at all of how modern materials such as plastics might fit into or challenge this history.296
âA Note on Metalâ is, quite literally, a prehistory, both in the conventional sense of dealing with the
prehistoric era, and to the extent that it constitutes a necessary prelude to the more comprehensive
re-writing that Prynne wishes to see.
v. Conclusion
In the fourth chapter of âJ.H. Prynne and Philologyâ, Sutherland provides a detailed account of the later
stages of Prynneâs relationshipâintellectual and personalâwith Charles Olson. Through a reading
of the correspondence and the later poems of The White Stones, he suggests that Prynneâs response
to Olsonâs 1953 prose work The Mayan Letters, reissued by Jonathan Cape in 1968, demonstrates a
fundamental difference between the two poetsâ conceptions of history. The Mayan Letters consists of
letters from Olson to Robert Creeley, written during a stay in Mexico and largely concerned with the
immediate apprehension of Mayan civilisation as a sort of pre-lapsarian state, to which modern society
might return through an act of poetic will. As is typical for Olson, despite its historical subject matter
and its engagement with primary sourcesâin this case, Mayan hieroglyphsâthe text deliberately
eschews all the trappings of academic history. As Sutherland puts it, describing the pamphlet The
Special View of History (1970), when Olson writes about history â[h]e means nothing about which we
can speak very precisely, and this is part of what he does mean.â297 Sutherland goes on to describe
Prynneâs poem âBronze : Fishâ, which he takes to be a direct response to The Mayan Letters, as âa
296Prynneâs explicit subject is, admittedly, metal; still, this does not prevent him from offering a detailed account of
metalâs relationship with stone.
297Sutherland, âJ.H. Prynne and Philologyâ, p. 189.
79
poetic argument for strictness and accuracy and against Olsonâs ânegativeâ capacity to romanticise
archaic cultures in disregard of scholarship.â298 His interpretation is supported by Prynneâs recent Paris
Review interview, in which he talks at length about Olsonâs aversion to serious scholarship, referring
directly to The Mayan Letters: âThe Mayan stuff, for example. [Olson] had no real understanding
of how glyph languages work. It was a romantic, liberational idea for him, but it took leave of the
historical record rather early and rather freely.â299
This distinction between Prynne and Olson sheds light, through Prynneâs attitude to history, on
âA Note on Metalâ. One of the key points that Sutherland uses to distinguish Prynne from Olson is
the formerâs emphasis on a history which persists, without fundamental breaks, from the time of the
historical object right up to the moment of composition. Whereas âOlson excavates back and down
into a world which is the contrary of modernityâ, Prynne remains committed to a theory of continuity,
however obscure and unrecognised. Nevertheless, Prynneâs position is also characterised by a deep lack
of certainty, an antipathy to settled and self-satisfied pronouncements about the relationship between
past and present, especially when made in the form of vatic utterances. This is precisely the scepticism
which, as was shown above, emerges in the final paragraph of âA Note on Metalâ. Sutherland derives a
political content from this stance, describing it as
a conscious refusal of specificity, on the grounds perhaps that both Poundâs and Olsonâs
seizure of specific cultural formations as the condition of unity led respectively to fascistic
and egregious forms of mythologisation of the heroic modern individual. Prynneâs vagueness
is thus a calculated form of resistance to political error, as well as a resistance to the
âtweezers and tropic cataloguesâ of academic forms of rationality cut off from any general
purpose or general understanding of their place in the world.300
For Sutherland, then, Prynne is both a proponent of accuracy and an opponent of specificity, depending
on the exact context of each work. As the contours of a text like âA Note on Metalâ make clear, Prynne
is prepared to deploy all the resources of conventional historical scholarship where they stand a real
chance of revealing something about our relation to the past, but he is careful to steer clear both of
pedantic scholarship-for-scholarshipâs-sake and of grand Olsonion/Poundian leaps which reject academic
rigour as a matter of myopic principle. This bears directly on the issue of a textâs poetic/prosaic status.
If, as Prynne claims in the 15 September letter to Riley, poems must be ârightâ and prose published in
little magazines should âless armour-platedâ, then any prose text worth readingâincluding âA Note
on Metalââmust in some sense be both poetry and prose. Good prose is not that which completely
dispenses with poetryâs immediate accuracy, but that which contains a central grain of it. At the same
time, the âpoeticisingâ of the text is in this case precisely what renders it âmore provisionalâ, opening up
chinks in its scholarly armour.

Some attention was paid at the beginning of this discussion to the distinctive philosophical vocabulary
deployed by Prynne in âA Note on Metalâ, and particularly to the term âsubstanceâ. It was suggested
that this word recalls the pantheism of Spinoza and the related idealism of Hegel, but that these
perspectives are entirely incommensurable with Prynneâs own, which emphasises the absolute exteriority
and self-sufficiency of the object, a category which includes ostensibly human creations such as language.
The key source for this attitude, particularly the latter part, is Prynneâs 1961 text âResistance and
Difficultyâ, a resumÃ© of a particular branch of the philosophy of perception whose main strand of
argument forms a dialogue with the American philosopher John Wildâs 1959 article âContemporary
Phenomenology and the Problem of Existenceâ.301 Wildâs key aim in this article is to defend the status
of what he sees as a form of direct, unmediated knowledge: âThe intentional act, together with its object,
is immediately self-revealing to me, and there is a clear sense in which I have a privileged access to such
intentions.â302 He also attempts to set up a distinction between reality and the imagination, taking
âresistanceâ to be a key marker of reality: âA real being not only resists my overt acts and manipulations;
it resists my acts of awareness as well. It reveals only a partial phase of itself in a perspective that
constantly changes with my movements.â303 An object of the imagination, by contrast, âis fixed, and
lacks this openness to further points of view.â304 Imaginative objects are âclear and distinctâ, while real
objects are âresistantâ.
It is this distinction with which Prynne takes issue. He begins by further distinguishing between
âresistanceâ and âdifficultyâ, the latter being the âsubjective counterpartâ of the former: âI experience
difficulty when I encounter resistance.â305 In other words, âdifficulty is cognitively prior to resistance
but ontologically dependent upon it.â306 Importantly, âresistance itself comes nearer than any other
differentiable quality to being completely inherent in the objectâ.307 He then demonstrates the âviciousnessâ
of a position like Wildâs, which âallows the external world only sufficient objective reality for the
major dimensions of human living to be set upâ:
âSelf-conscious actionâ in the milieu described with such eloquence, as a primary source for
the experience of existence, seems to point to a position where the external worldâs main
value to the subject lies in the graduated difficulties that it can provide him with, and by
means of which the subject can render himself and his actions fully intelligible.308
Wildâs stance is reduced to a sort of weak (in the intellectual sense) epistemological solipsism, albeit

311 In this short work, Meillassoux takes aim at a stance which he
terms âcorrelationismâ, and which he takes to be the dominant perspective in contemporary philosophy,

what he terms, after Francis Wolff, a âtransparent cageâ:

some extent an âinside jobâ, accepting the premises of his opponent before adding a new term which
completely reconfigures the situation; unlike Prynneâs, it is strictly rationalist in character. The details
of his argument, which relies largely on the counterintuitive implications of absolute contingency, are,
however, of less interest in the present context than his reasons for making it. Meillassoux sees the main
problem with correlationism not in the theory itself, but in what its abdication of responsibility for the
absolute makes room for: a new fideism. For the strong correlationist, reason has no more privileged
access to the absolute than does faithâany claim to such access is equally valid. This tendency is
referred to by Meillassoux as the âreligionizing of reasonâ, and he sees it as having operated ever since
the Enlightenmentâs first critiques of religious dogmatism.324 He demonstrates how, in the modern
era, philosophers as diverse as Wittgenstein and Heidegger have allowed fideistic elements into their
work, giving Levinasâs conception of âthe wholly otherâ as a paradigmatic example of twentieth-century
religionised reason.325
Meillassouxâs opposition to fideism seems, at the very least, to be structurally analogous to Prynneâs

antipathy towards the supposedly direct historical access represented by Olsonâs Mayan Letters. Whereas
for Meillassoux what needs to be countered is the attitude which accords legitimacy to protestations of

assigns a privileged epistemological status to the poet while evacuating the latter term of meaning:
the poet just has access to the essence of Mayan civilisation, without having to do any (or having
to do only minimal) poetic work. It is clear, however, that the two situations are not exactly the
same. In fact, it is necessary at this point to distinguish between two separate comparisons between
Prynne and Meillassoux, which are not entirely compatible. In âResistance and Difficultyâ, Prynne
takes aim at a philosophical position that falls into the broad category of correlationism, with the aim,
like Meillassoux, of reopening a channel to the âgreat outdoorsâ of reality; in his comments on Olson,
as described by Sutherland, he expresses scepticism about an attitude to history comparable to what
Meillassoux characterises as fideism. Yet for Prynne these two undesirable positions do not seem to
exist in a toxic relationship of complementarity-in-difference. It is not the case that John Wildâs stance
leaves a gap which is filled by Olsonâs; or at least, if it is, this is not the line taken by Prynne. In
fact, the solution to the problem of correlationism provided in âResistance and Difficultyâ seems, at
first glance, closer to fideism than to Meillassouxâs âspeculative materialismââit is certainly difficult to
imagine the latter, for all his commitment to poetry, being satisfied by such a simple move.326 Equally,
the predominant âtweezers and tropic cataloguesâ approach to history opposed by Prynne can hardly
be considered a form of correlationism, its disjunction from reality being a function of its academic
self-absorption rather than a deliberate epistemological stance.
This clarification of Prynneâs twin positions offers a new way of looking at his task in âA Note on
Metalâ. On the one hand, it is a text which does real historical work based on archaeological evidence,
and which refuses to claim some sort of poetic position automatically superior to the academic books
and articles which are cited in its endnotes. This is the level on which the text was approached in the
early stages of this reading, accepting its claim to empirical knowledge and testing that claim against
more recent evidence where necessary. On the other hand, âA Note on Metalâ is in some sense a poetic
work. The argument for considering the text as a âprose poemâ based on its publication in Poems
was dismissed earlier as overbroad. Nevertheless, the very fact that âA Note on Metalâ is so regularly
confused with a poem is itself suggestive, and means that it does differ from something like the bookâs
index of first lines. The word ânoteâ in the title of such a hugely ambitious text must be consciously
ironic, but it is also a key term for Prynne, deployed to describe other prose worksââA Pedantic
Note in Two Partsâ, Field Notesâas well as the poem âFirst Notes on Daylightâ, which is roughly
contemporaneous with âA Note on Metalâ. For Prynne, the note is a form whose apparent modesty
also implies flexibility and freedom from the more teleologically determinant aspects of academic
326Meillassouxâs two book-length publications subsequent to After FinitudeâLe Nombre et la sirÃ¨ne (2011) and
MÃ©taphysique et fiction des mondes hors-science (2013)âboth concern literature.
85
rationality, while maintaining some of the more useful, lower-level aspects: scholarly accuracy, concern
for references, and so on. Like reports in Notes and Queries, Prynneâs notes are freed from the formal
and argumentative imperatives of the full-length journal article or the scholarly monograph; yet unlike
such reports, they make use of this freedom to head in a direction of their own. If âA Note on Metalâ is
to be read accurately, it must be read not only as a work of history, but also as one of those âentities
which subsequently become a part of the worldâ which are described in âResistance and Difficultyââthat
is to say, as a poem.
The poem, for Prynne, is perhaps something like a reverse arche-fossil. Whereas Meillassouxâs
arche-fossil is an object completely external to humanity which is nevertheless recuperable by science
(it is this recuperability which creates problems for correlationism), the poemâa category which
includes âA Note on Metalââis a man-made object characterised by its having become at least partially
unrecuperable: once written, it is a resistant piece of the external world, whose essence can never be
entirely captured by hermeneutics. An important consequence of this view in the present context is that
the poem thereby becomes part of history, a data point on the same ontological plane as any other piece
of historical evidence.327 âHistoryâ is a word with at least two meanings: it designates both the object
of historical enquiryâin the most basic sense, facts about the pastâand, as âa historyâ, the process
of recovering, synthesising and recounting those facts, i.e. the act of historical enquiry itself. âA Note
on Metalâ is history in both senses, and the careful and deliberate nature of its textual construction
suggests that it is aware of this status. This is true not only of âA Note on Metalâ as a whole, but of
each of its constituent parts, right down to individual words. We thus return to the phenomenon by
which the text is a sort of index of itself, described earlier as an attempt to make clear the conditions
of possibility of its own terminology. In being simultaneously a history and a poemâin other words,
resistant, requiring an effort of interpretation, not self-effacing or aiming at transparency like a more
conventional piece of historical discourseââA Note on Metalâ is something different from epic, in which
the poem merely includes history as its object. It is here that Prynneâs âexilic (left-wing) history of
substanceâ can be found. Paradoxically, it is only when the text is read as history and nothing more
that this deeper history appears âexilicâ: when it is grasped in its full substantiality, as history and
poem, it appears clearly, not as the object of the text but as the substance from which every word is
formed, as well as the animating principle behind their configuration.328 It is as if âA Note on Metalâ
was an impossible sort of coin, one side of which precludes access to its obverse, while the other allows
both to be viewed at once. What, then, does it actually take to view the text, and history as such,
from this privileged angle?

The temporary suspension of accuracy in this world is the condition for accuracy in the next, which is

humility, is perhaps to effect or at least to encourage some form of the âswing across into | some other
version of this | present ageâ, manifested in this particular case by a shift from the historical to the
poetic-as-historical levels of the text. Only with this final acknowledgement of ignorance, both on the
part of Prynne and of the reader, does the text yield up its real meaning.

Chapter 3. âThe idea of perfectionâ
i. Introduction
The preceding chapters introduced contexts for Prynneâs career through consideration of his critical,
theoretical and historical prose. It was pointed out that Prynne is not merely a poet, though this
is undoubtedly the work for which he is most well-known, but also a critic, a teacher and an editor,
and that this variety of activities is reflected in the various pieces of prose work that he produced
between the mid 1950s and the mid 1970s. The present chapter will see a change both in content and
in methodology, considering Prynneâs relationship to a form thatâleaving aside an arguable exception
which will be considered in the fifth section of this chapterâhe has never actually practised: fiction.
This does not, however, mean that the argument will be unable to draw upon primary texts by Prynne.
While Prynne has never writtenâor, at least, never publishedâconventional prose fiction himself, he
has certainly produced texts which comment on the fiction of others, often in great detail. These most
often take the form of private letters, either to the subjects of the commentary or to third parties,
but Prynne has also published at least one formal review of a novel, as well as public letters and less
formal comments. Beyond assessing Prynneâs attitude to fiction in general, this chapter will investigate
a number of similarities between prose texts that Prynne has professed to admire, proposing a third
context for his work, akin to the nexus of Leavisite criticism and Movement poetry discussed in Chapter
1 or the avant-garde little magazine scene explored in the first half of Chapter 2. It will go on to draw
broader conclusions about Prynneâs basic ethical and aesthetic stance, building on the reading of âA
Note on Metalâ offered in the second half of that chapter. Specifically, a gap will be traced between two
styles in Prynneâs prose workâone reasoned and deliberate, the other vitriolic and dismissiveâwhich
will then be reconnected through their shared advocacy of a number of implicitly aesthetic qualities,
most notably sexual maturity.
The list of novelists mentioned by Prynne in his letters of the 1960s and â70s is long, but not
perhaps as long as might be expected; certainly, it is dwarfed by the number of poets mentioned.
This manageability makes it relatively easy to identify authors who appear more than once, or with
whom Prynne corresponded directly, suggesting that he was particularly interested in their work. This
restricted list may be divided into three main categories: Prynneâs avant-garde contemporaries in
Britain (Tony Ward, Douglas Oliver); American writers associated loosely with the Black Mountain
group (Ed Dorn, Douglas Woolf); and older figures with whom Prynne is unlikely to have had any
direct personal connection (Wyndham Lewis, Edward Upward). In-keeping with this thesisâs focus on
British contexts for Prynneâs work, this chapter will concentrate on the first and the third of these
groups, leaving aside Tony Ward, whose novels are generally only mentioned by Prynne in passing, or in
88
conversations about the practicalities of publication.330 The relationship between the three remaining
namesâWyndham Lewis, Douglas Oliver and Edward Upwardâis, it will be argued, roughly triangular,
in the sense that there are a number of strong similarities between Lewis and Upward, Upward and
Oliver, and Oliver and Lewis, but only weaker ones between all three figures at once. In some cases,
the relationship is one of direct influence: Upward has stated explicitly, albeit with prompting, that
âWyndham Lewis [. . . ] influenced me considerablyâ, and a very specific connection will be made between
sections of Upwardâs and Oliverâs work.331 In others, the link rests more on the shared use of certain
literary techniques. Through an examination of Prynneâs recorded comments on each author, the initial
sections of this chapter will attempt to clarify both this structure and the relationship between Prynne
and prose fiction to which it corresponds.
ii. Wyndham Lewis

iii. Douglas Oliver
First published as a joint venture by Andrew Crozierâs Ferry Press and John Riley and Tim Longvilleâs
Grosseteste Review Books in 1973, The Harmless Building was reissued in revised form in the 1990
Paladin collection Three Variations on the Theme of Harm. It is Douglas Oliverâs only individuallyauthored
prose novel. To an even greater extent than Tarr, The Harmless Building is structured around

elements and by the relationships and personal concerns of its three main characters: Donald (the
ostensible protagonist), Rosine and Frank. Chief among these concerns is the kidnapping of a âmongolâ
(in the archaic sense referring to Downâs syndrome) baby named Uncle Aubrey, a catastrophe which is
accompanied by a serious railway accident and a hotel fire. Again like Tarr, The Harmless Building
was written during or shortly after a prolonged stay in Paris, and shows a deep but undeferential
concern with the latest developments in European, and especially French, intellectual culture. Chapter
12 begins with âseagulls circl[ing] overhead, calling âJacques Lacan, Jacques Lacanâ, without giving
further details.â351

Before examining this sexualised approach to culture in relation to Edward Upward, it will be

and body, before the whole shape is extruded along the axis of time, creating a tubular structure.
It is not clear why Prynne uses the term âhypersurfaceâ, which refers to a surface with one fewer
dimensions than the space in which it exists (e.g. a one-dimensional point on a two-dimensional plane,
or a two-dimensional plane in a three-dimensional space). More interesting is the word âendocrineâ,
which re-emphasises the bodily nature of the structure: different points in the novel are linked to each
other in an arrangement analogous to the bodyâs endocrine system, in which glands transfer information
via hormones secreted into the blood.

Prynneâs comments on Oliverâs work, along with the negative terms against which he defines its
aesthetic achievement, helpfully illuminate his intellectual relationship with Lewis. Literary history is
never a zero-sum game; nevertheless, Prynneâs selection of Lewis as his favoured âman of 1914â is bound
to have negative as well as positive implications, the more so given the note of conspiracy with which it
is made: if Lewis has been unfairly neglected, who has been stealing the limelight? The obvious culprit
is James Joyce. In many ways, Prynneâs praise of The Harmless Building corresponds in an inverted
fashion to Lewisâs attempted demolition of Joyceâhis sometime friend but long-term artistic rivalâin
the 1926 essay âThe Revolutionary Simpletonâ, later reprinted as part of Time and Western Man (1927).
Lewis uses Joyce as an exemplar in fiction of the âtime-cultâ which it is the main purpose of Time
and Western Man to counter, and deploys a wide and sometimes contradictory range of arguments to
make his point. He begins with a straightforward denial of Joyceâs intellectual capacity, presenting
him as an unwitting vehicle for the âtime-machineryâ derived from Bergson: âthere is not very much
reflection going on at any time inside the head of Mr. James Joyce. That is indeed the characteristic
condition of the craftsman, pure and simple. [. . . H]e is not so much an inventive intelligence as an
executant.â359 Joyceâs work, for Lewis, is fatally concerned with styleâan odd contention for a writer
whose own fiction is now primarily read for its stylistic innovations, but one which Lewis pursues
doggedly.360 Joyce might indeed be described as the exemplar par excellence of what Prynne disparages
as âmicrorhetoricâ: a concern for languageâs musicality over its meaning.
On a more fundamental level, Joyceâs workâand particularly Ulyssesâis criticised for its reliance
on time as a structuring principle, as against Lewisâs preferred metaphor, space. In a 1963 letter to
Olson, Prynne shows his clear appreciation of this view, quoting at length from Time and Western

to âBergsonâs musicalised worldâ, describing the process of successful poetic creation as a deliberate
neutralisation of time: âtime is a governing absolute within the process, the immersal in the poem and
its coming to be; but the achieved kinetics of the thing on the page are tensions, which is the idiom
of poem as construct or diagram rather than passage or transit.â361 Assuming something similar may
be said to operate in the composition of novels, Prynneâs description of The Harmless Building as a
disputable, insofar as he describes âChapters 11 to 15 as texte, what follows as hors-texteâ, while the section of direct
address described above arguably falls within Chapter 15 in both published versions of the novel.

direction: âattracted by the lure of the âimmoralâ, everybody in the end will be induced to become
more moral, simply-clothed, well-behaved and inexpensive.â366 Ulysses is no more than a pawn in this

Lewis is not the only literary-philosophical reference point used to describe Oliverâs work. Oliverâs

passing reference to Iris Murdoch: âit is the risk throughout which I find profoundly moving. Going right
through to where the heart would be if the reader could afford that golden idea-rhyme; I think of Iris
Murdochâs paper on âThe Idea of Perfectionâ in her new book The Sovereignty of Good.â In this paper,
first published in The Yale Review in 1964, Murdoch attempts to counter a âbehaviourist, existential,
and utilitarianâ model of ethics which she considers to be pervasive in British philosophy departments,
and whose influence she attributes to G.E. Moore, though her immediate target is her former Oxford
colleague Stuart Hampshire.369 Central to this attitude is the idea that we relate to the good not
primarily through a process analogous to seeing (introspectively or otherwise) but through actions
in the world. Allied to this is the rule that âmental concepts must be analyzed geneticallyââthrough
back-reference from the actions that they occasionââand so the inner must be thought of as parasitic
on the outer.â370 Applying this rule rigorously leads to a situation in which the concept of a private
decision either ceases to be relevant or ceases to exist altogether: âA decision does not turn out to be
[. . . ] an introspectible moment. The concept has no further inner structure; it is its outer structure.â371
Murdoch counters this focus on action by means of an example. M, a mother, dislikes D, her
daughter-in-law. M nevertheless behaves perfectly, not allowing her feelings to show, and D subsequently
disappears from the scene. M continues to think about D and gradually changes her opinion. No
subsequent action on Mâs part results from this changed opinion. From the behaviourist-existentialistutilitarian
standpoint, this state of affairs can only have meaning in a hypothetical sense: âMâs vision of
D has altered means that if M were to speak her mind about D now she would say different things
from the things she would have said three years ago.â372 But, as Murdoch points out, â[t]he truth of the
hypothetical proposition could be consistent with nothing in the interim having occurred in Mâs mind
at allâ, while in this particular case something clearly has occurred.373 Murdoch spends the remainder
of the paper in an attempt to rehabilitate metaphors of visionâand particularly of attention, a concept
which she takes from Simone Weilâin moral discourse: âWhat M is ex hypothesi attempting to do is
not just to see D accurately but to see her justly or lovingly. [. . . ] Freedom is not the sudden jumping
of the isolated will in and out of an impersonal logical complex, it is a function of the progressive
attempt to see the object clearly.â374 Crucially, she goes on to connect this ethical stance, in which

Returning to Prynneâs comments on The Harmless Building, it is now possible to interpret his

superset ofâloving a person. Prynne has already made clear that he interprets The Harmless Building

system. It is only logical that such a body would have a heart, the organ responsible for circulating
that systemâs hormones, even if only as an âidea-rhymeâ. While the casual reader may not always be
able to âaffordâ the level of attention that this model implies, it is nevertheless real, and is a result of

all along; you knew that. I love you.â377 In fact, it is not necessary to specify a single object, providing
that Oliver relates to the novelâs series of objects in the mode of ânecessityâ and âobedienceâ specified
by Murdoch. âAttention worked out so closely does not often attain to a condition of truthâ, writes
Prynne, implying that, in this case at least, it does.378
iv. Edward Upward
In his remarks to Douglas Oliver, just as in his comments to Peter Riley on Wyndham Lewis, Prynne
makes use of age and sexual maturity as motifs to distinguish avant-garde English writing from its
French and American equivalents. Whether describing the latter as âadolescentâ and âpre-sexualâ or as
an âold-fashionedâ art practised by âold granniesâ, the implication is that its English counterpart is in
the prime of life, dealing with subjects which are appropriate to a mature tradition in forms which are
equally appropriate. This chapter will now move to consider a more controversial case, which will test

This is a relatively conventional description of Upwardâs position in British literary culture in the
mid 1970s.380 Like Wyndham Lewis, little more than two decades his senior, Upward has been
overshadowed by more famous friends and associates, most notably his close friend at school and
university, Christopher Isherwood. This neglect is generally attributed to a long period of literary
silence coinciding with his membership of the British Communist Party: Upward published no major
work between the Second World War and the early 1960s, when In the Thirties (1962), the first volume
of the trilogy which includes The Rotten Elements, was printed.381 Shoring up this characterisation,
the semi-autobiographical trilogyâpublished in full as The Spiral Ascent in 1977âtakes the conflict
between its protagonistâs political commitments and his literary ambitions as its central theme. In
the Thirties describes how a young, middle-class teacher and poet, Alan Sebrill, joins the Party after
a period of creative despair. Sebrill struggles at length to reconcile the three competing demands of
political work, âthe poetic lifeâ and his day job, while becoming increasingly disenchanted with what he
sees as a drift away from orthodox Leninism by the Party leadership following the war. His attempts
to counter this tendency lead to his expulsion, along with his wife, an event which forms the pivotal
moment of The Rotten Elements.
382 In the final volume, No Home But the Struggle, a retired Sebrill
reflects simultaneously on his childhood and on his current involvement with the Campaign for Nuclear
Disarmament (CND). Towards the end of the novel, he finally reaches a state of reconciliation between
poetry and politics, and the book ends self-referentially with Upwardâs stand-in having completed his
own great work, âthe poem which I have been working on for six years and have written the final lines
of todayâ.383

Divorced from its context, this passage risks taking on an ironic tone which it does not necessarily
possess in the original narrative, where a line like âYou always were a loyal feminist, dearâ is actually
representative of the way Upwardâs characters speak. Taken at face value, then, the message is one of
seemingly justified scepticism about the role of sexual experimentation in revolutionary practiceâwhile
it may have its place in a communist future, Alan and Elsie seem to agree, it is not properly part
of the preparations for that future. While desire is sensibly acknowledged in the form of Sebrillâs
âsentimental hankeringâ, it is quickly repressed, not (supposedly) by bourgeois sexual morality, but by
reason: even the utopian description of sexual relations under âfull communismâ is conducted in logical
terms, its arrangement justified as a system of eminently sensible âcompensationsâ. This acceptance

whole rear of the train had reprehensibly failed in its direction. Donald soon realized that
his journey had simply crocked its elbow. He could dimly see the tragedy like a hill in his
past. The two central carriages had remarkably telescoped, a time-juncture; they had torn
each otherâs metal aside in eagerness to come together and had reared up in excitement.

like a dog no longer interested in the nightâs orgy. Donaldâs famous locust gesture came
upon him again, cracking his joints, which exuded darkness as the carriages cocked up and

Perhaps the most obvious points of comparison here are the twin sexual metaphors, both expressed
through reference to animals: where Upwardâs carriages are âviciously copulating bullsâ, Oliverâs engine
is a sexually sated dog. Two writers recounting similar events might be expected to hit upon similar
metaphors, yet Oliver and Upward also deploy identical terminology: both, for instance, describe how
the carriages âtelescopedâ on impact.

The Harmless Building and Prynneâs letter to Oliver, as well as sharing a publisher with the latter
two.411 Early ideas for the text had been worked out in a series of letters to Ed Dorn, which appeared
in modified form as reports in Bean News in 1972.412 The PTM is presented for the most part as
an account of a scientific conference on the âPlant Time Manifoldâ of its title, which can be roughly
summarised as the idea that plantsâ bi-directional vertical movement (roots downward, shoots upward)
implies the need for a new model of time. Held in London and extending from 1 April at the latest
to 3 May 1972, the conference is fictional, as are its participants, who are given the names of plants:
Professor Lichen, Dr Cypress, Dr Gale. Nevertheless, the academic papers and books to which these
characters refer in their reported presentations are invariably real, with scrupulous citations and easily
traceable quotes. The PTM thus exists in a liminal space between veracious academic discourse and
fiction, a characteristic which will be considered below.
Whereas Justin Katko, in the most detailed account to date, reads the PTM on the most literal
level, taking the text as one which âknows itself to constitute actual scientific thinkingâ, this reading
will pay deliberate attention to its fictional aspects, something which scholars of Prynneâs work have so
far been reluctant to do.413 This is not to contradict Katkoâs strongly argued claim that the science of
the work should be taken seriously, but it is to temper his subsequent contention that âany projection
of irony or satire onto the core propositions of this treatise would be detrimental to a clear-eyed grasp
of the hypothesisâ.414 While we might well agree that any such âprojectionâ would be unwarranted,
it is possible to deny that what other critics have performed is a projection at all. Real science
may be embedded within a fictional frame, and the frame itself may be worthy of analysis, but its
position as a frame does not separate it irremediably from that which it contains. As Katko points
out, referring to one of the original Bean News reports which formed the basis of the authoritative
text published in Grosseteste Review, âthe satirical frame is not locked in until the end of âFull Tilt
Botany,â where we enter the diegetic framework of the actual PTM conferenceââin other words, the
initial stages of the text are presented more or less transparently as Prynneâs own discourse, or at least
as that of his pseudonym, Erasmus W. Darwin, who shares a first name and surname with Charles
Darwinâs grandfather.415 Nevertheless, the decision to expand the frame in the compiled version,
beginning the text as reportageââTodayâs session of the London PTM Conferenceââclearly authorises
the interpretation of the whole work as fictional, if only as one reading among others.416

Perhaps the most obvious sense in which the PTM may be compared to the work of Lewis, Oliver and
Upward is in its use of humour. However serious its scientific content, the PTM is a fundamentally comic
text; Drew Milne goes so far as to claim that it âcontains moments of undergraduate knockaboutâ.417
Crucially, the methods through which this humour is generated share much with those of the writers
considered above. Prynneâs characters, for instance, are completely lacking in depth: like Lewisâs
Breton peasants in The Wild Body, they are âpuppetsâ, shaped by their environmentâin this case, the
academic conferenceârather than shaping it. For the most part, figures like Professor Quondam Lichen
are relatively transparent mouthpieces for the monophonic scientific discourse that makes up the bulk of
the PTM. Rather than being given verbatim, their papers are narrated in the third person by the textâs
ostensible author, a scientific journalist who, while not strictly extradiegetic, nevertheless refrains from
intervening in any of the action. At one important point, however, the charactersâ speech is reported
directly. As this is the most conventionally fictional part of the text, it will be worth considering in
detail.
The section follows Lichenâs initial paper and is presented as a transcript from a seminar attended by
Lichen, Cypress and âa small group of co-workersâ following âa prolonged lunchâ.418 Cypress, ârelax[ing]
the formalitiesâ, asks Lichen a question about âroot uptake of organic compounds in the, er, higher
plantsâ, a hierarchical term at which Lichenânot being a âhigher plantâ himselfâtakes offence.419
Lichen embarks on a long and increasingly ill-tempered rant, with Cypress attempting to intervene,
before âcollaps[ing] into a rosette on the deskâ, having âduring the previous (âconvivialâ) lunch taken
in a good deal of liquid refreshment.â420 Of the texts considered above, the one which this section
most closely resembles is Upwardâs âThe Railway Accidentâ. Like most of Upwardâs Mortmere stories,
it is dialogue-heavy, involving a rapid back-and-forth between two characters (though unlike parts of
âThe Railway Accidentâ, Prynne avoids confusion by repeating the charactersâ names in the dialogue:
âWell, Dr Cypressâ; âOh indeed yes, Professor Lichenâ).421 The characters themselves, though nominally
plants, are recognisably upper-middle class Englishmen of exactly the sort that populate Mortmere, and
as in many of the Mortmere storiesâthough not âThe Railway Accidentââtheir conversation is given
added momentum by the intoxication of the one of the parties. Most importantly, the dialogue follows
the characteristically Mortmerian structure according to which a seemingly benign surface narrative
conceals an obscure and potentially violent undercurrent. In fact, this is made clear by the narrator,
for whom âthe incident [. . . ] remains indicative of important biometric tensions underlying this London
conference.â422

An even stronger resemblance can be found between this particular section of the PTM and a work
which falls firmly outside the nexus represented by Lewis, Oliver and Upward, but which forms part
of the broader cultural formation analysed in Chapter 1. It would be misleading to characterise the
PTM solely as academic satire: while the conference setting is important, it remains a frame for the
action which takes place within it. Even so, and despite its avant-garde credentials, it seems clear that
the PTM owes something to the British campus novel, two prominent examples of whichâKingsley
Amisâs Lucky Jim (1954) and David Lodgeâs Changing Places (1975)âsit roughly at either end of
this thesisâs chronological scope. The most instructive point of comparison is Chapter 22 of Amisâs
novel, which forms the comic climax of the book. Having repeatedly steadied his nerves with whisky
and sherry, the protagonist, Jim Dixonâa member of academic staff at a provincial universityâgives
a public lecture on the topic âmerrie Englandâ. Just as in the PTM, the lecture begins relatively
normally but quickly degenerates. Where Lichenâs descent into drunkenness is signified by orthography
(âscr-r-rupulousâ, âHoots monâ, âcrowlinâ creaturesâ, âobsairvedâ), Dixonâs is described directly: âAlmost
unconsciously he began to adopt an unnameable foreign accent and to read faster and faster, his head
spinning. [. . . ] He began punctuating his discourse with smothered snorts of derision. He read on,
spitting out the syllables like curses, leaving mispronunciations, omissions, spoonerisms uncorrectedâ.423
Nevertheless, intoxication is in both cases signified by changes in speech. Dixon does not finally collapse
like Lichen, but it is made clear that he is not far from it (âHe paused and swayed; the heat, the
drink, the nervousness, the guilt at last joined forces in himâ), and his behaviour is certainly âindicative
of important [. . . ] tensionsââprincipally his fraught relationship with his senior colleague, Professor
Welch, whose voice Dixon accidentally imitates in the course of the lecture.424
Before connecting the PTM more directly to the work of Oliver and Lewis, it is worth considering
the academic aspect of the text in more detail. As noted above, the conference frame is incidental
to the thesis that sits at the heart of the text, but it is not therefore arbitrary. Prynne has spent
almost his entire literary career in an academic environmentâan environment towards which he has
entertained considerable ambivalence, as the latter part of Chapter 1 sought to demonstrate. The
university and its associated institutions constitute the field of production of virtually all of Prynneâs
poetry and prose. Why, then, does it take until 1974 for this field to become visible in the work itself?
To put it another way, what is it about the precise period in which the PTM was composed that allows

Writing to Dorn again in 1975, in a notably untidy hand, Prynne gives a powerful account of the

a.m., itâs a treadmill [. . . ].426
Married with two young children and having recently bought a house, it is perhaps not so difficult to see
why Prynne would in his mid-to-late thirties emphasise maturity as an aesthetic criterion. Importantly,
the integration into the structures of bourgeois society represented by these personal milestones comes
at the same time as what is arguably Prynneâs tightest professional integration into the university.
As a student and later as a research fellow at Caius, Prynneâs poetic identity threatened to eclipse
his academic one: whatever his later assessment of Force of Circumstance, to have a debut collection
issued by a major national publishing house at the age of 26 is a significant achievement. The same is
obviously true of the later stages of Prynneâs career, certainly since the publication of the first edition
of his collected poems in 1982, in which his increasing literary fame has far outstripped any academic
advancement (Prynne has never, for instance, held the title of professor at Cambridge, though his
poetic work has led to honorary and visiting professorial appointments at the University of Sussex and
at Sun Yat-sen University in China). The 1970s, however, represent a window between these two stages.
Not only was Prynne given an official university position in this period, but through Caius he was
on close termsâprofessionally if not always personallyâwith some of the most important academic,
and especially scientific, figures of the period: notably Stephen Hawking, whose Large Scale Structure
of Space-Time was published in 1973, and the Nobel laureate Francis Crick. The biochemist and
sinologist Joseph Needham, a close friend of Prynneâs, served as Master of the college from 1966â76,
affording him further influence. In satirising the politics and personality clashes of contemporary
academic science, then, Prynne may well have been drawing on personal experience of a relatively
recent character. Indeed, it could easily be the case that the PTMâs sophisticated engagement with
modern biology and physics would not have been possible had it been composed in a less academically
diverse environment than a Cambridge college. Some of the specific results of this diversity will be
considered in the conclusion to this chapter.
To return to the PTMâs fictional qualities, what is it that links the text to the work of Oliver
425Prynne to Dorn, 10 January 1972.
426Prynne to Dorn, 5 March 1975.
111
and Lewis, its âMortmerianâ character having already been considered? The key point of connection,
which gives rise to a number of subconnections, is the textâs concern with the body. It was argued
above that both Lewis and Oliver strive continually to defamiliarise the human body. This is done
primarily through the means of metaphor (Lewisâs âcreaking men-machinesâ, Oliverâs ice cream cornets
and penknives), with the body as tenor and some conventionally inanimate object as vehicle. Beyond
defamiliarisation, the fundamental effect is to remove the barrier between the body and its other: the
body becomes a simple part of the material world, just like anything else. At times, however, this
metaphorical structure breaks down into something less rigid. This can be seen clearly in the second
quotation from Oliver given above, in which the body is said to resemble âthree ramsâ heads on a
totem-poleâ. While this introduction obeys the conventional arrangement of tenor and vehicle, what
follows does not, as the body is divided into three faces, comprising the characterâs actual face, his
torso and his genitals. Comparing a face to a face is relatively straightforward, but doing the same
with a face and a torso or a face and genitalsâseparate parts of the same bodyâis more complex.
As a metaphorical operation, it may be considered the reverse of that for which Prynne defended
Shelley in his reply to Leavis, analysed in Chapter 1, though it is by no means less disruptive of
conventional sense-making. Rather than forgetting the tenor in a too-eager concern for the properties
of the metaphorical vehicle, Oliver sticks rigidly to the former, seeking his vehicle in the tenor itself.
This is achieved through successive metonymic expansions (from the torso and genitals to the body
as a whole) and contractions (from the body back down to the face). In the case of the torso-face
comparison, this operation works along a pre-existing aesthetic path laid down by such works as the
paintings in RenÃ© Magritteâs Le Viol (âThe Rapeâ) series.427
The metaphorical implications of the PTM with regard to the human body similarly fail to conform
to the simple animate/inanimate defamiliarisation structure. In Prynneâs text, plants take the place
of humans, but this is by no means a simple process of substitution. In fact, the PTM derives much
of its comic force precisely from its failure seamlessly to map tenor on to vehicle, leaving the reader
in a sort of no-manâs-land between the two. As mentioned above, the most important clue to the
textâs metaphorical structure is provided by the charactersâ names: Lichen, Cypress, âMyrtleâ Gale. Yet
Prynne allows the reader to take in four lengthy paragraphs of Professor Quondam Lichenâs reported
paper before his colleague, Cypress, is introduced, making it difficult to pick up on the joke from the
very beginning. Moreover, the use of a mixed bag of species (Gale, referring to Myrica gale), plant
family (Cypress) and organism (Lichen) names in place of personal names creates further confusion:
are these characters to be understood as individual plants, or as representatives of their hierarchically
distinct botanical categories? Is it significant that the character with the highest academic honours,
Lichen, represents the most conceptually broad category, or that the only character definitely identified

as a woman represents the narrowest? What is the significance of sexual identification at allâincluding,

To ask such questions is not to âover-readâ an essentially comic text, but rather to treat the PTMâs
fictional frame with the same seriousness that critics have used in analysing its scientific content.
To understand whether or not the characters in the PTM have bodies in a conventional sense,
it is helpful to turn once again to the seminar scene discussed above. Beginning at the conclusion
of Lichenâs paper, this scene includes the first significant use of verbs to describe charactersâ actions,
the only previous instance being the statement in the introductory paragraph that the paper was
âdelivered by Professor Quondam Lichenâ.430 The paper having been delivered, Lichen is greeted with
âpolite, photosynthetic applause.â431 Immediately, plant (photosynthesis) and human (applause) are
confused, with no guidance given on how to interpret the resulting compound, though the mention
of photosynthesis perhaps suggests plants clapping their leaves. The narrator makes reference to a
âprolonged lunchâ preceding the seminar, but the characters are never described eating and drinking
directly, leaving the exact nature of their physical embodiment mysterious. In the course of Lichenâs
drunken rant, the pendulum seems to swing from human back to plant action: defending the status of
lichens as a class, the professor describes âseeing trees held up by their Lichen canopiesâ, a statement
in which the capitalisation of âlichenâ reintroduces the problems of naming which have already been
described.432 Dismissing carrots, he further alleges that âthose crowlinâ creatures will metabolise
anything, phagocytic riff-raffâ, a statement which obviously takes its points of reference from the plant
kingdom, despite humans also being able to âmetaboliseâ.433 Finally, Lichenâs collapse after having
âtaken in a good deal of liquid refreshmentâ is clearly meant to recall alcoholic intoxication, yet the
lack of an explicit statement to that effect leaves open the possibility of a more plant-appropriate
explanation.434

Prynne uses youth and maturity as oppositional terms in his discussion of writers such as Lewis
and Oliver, painting American poets as âart-gallery nympholeptsâ concerned only with a âpre-sexual
phenomenologyâ that is essentially, and unfortunately, French. This national coding ties into a broader
structure counterposing solid, serious work and frivolous artificiality, which can be detected as the
basis of many comments in Prynneâs letters in this period. Writing to the American poet Robin Blaser
in 1973, for instance, with a stinging critique of his work, Prynne refers to âthe fashionable French
intellectual, more than content with the sawdust of rhetoric in place of the true honie & gall on the lips
of the poetââa poet who, as the archaic orthography of the proverbial âhoney and gallâ suggests, is
English by default.443 There seems at first glance to be a ready-made counterpart to this structure in
the opposition between natural growth and grafting. Natural growth shares all the qualities implicitly
promoted by Prynne: it is the product of sexual reproduction among mature plants whose physical
proximity ensures a certain level of indigeneity. Grafting, by contrast, involves the eminently artificial
importation of âforeignâ shoots (within certain genetic limits) with the explicit purpose of rendering
sexual reproduction unnecessary. Thus, while grafted plants are not strictly âpre-sexualâ in the sense
that they are unable to reproduce, what they exhibit is a sort of fast-tracked or counterfeit maturity.
Moreover, if a process of continual regrafting such as that seemingly described in Sonnet 15 takes
place, then they will not even need to sexually reproduce themselves. The connection is more than
conceptual: at one point in the text, Prynne notes that â[i]n earlier literary usage, often allegorical, the
characteristic term for scion is ympeâ, a word with a pleasing similarity, if not a direct etymological
connection, to nymph.

Which doâs mend Nature: change it rather, but

The supposedly persuasive appeal to tradition in this argument in fact renders it unconvincing, not
least to its intended fictional recipient, Perdita, who as Prynne notes âfinds it to be freakish and
contaminated [and] refuses to accept the act of grafting as safely within the permitted and natural
order of things.â446 Through a consideration of the gradual accretion of pejorative connotations around
the word âconceitâ, however, Prynne reveals the complex and multiple layers of artificiality which make
up such tropes: âgrafting was recognised as a curious, subtle and conceited action [. . . ]; in figurative
contexts this element of conceitfulness is compounded, since the trope is itself a product of inventive
conceit and (by extension) is itself termed âa conceit.â â447 The end result of this spiral of artificiality
is to reveal how the system extends in both directions: if the use of grafting as a way to describe
artificiality is a conceit, then the bias towards nature is itself artificial, if not in its origin then certainly
in its persistence. The maintenance of a distinction between nature and artificeâa precondition of
the privileging of natureâis dependent on rhetorical and metaphorical techniques which are just as
constructed as those used in an argument such as Polixenesâs.
vi. Conclusion
When Graft and Corruption is added to the group of non-fictional texts which have already been
considered in this chapterâprincipally letters from Prynneâa gap seems to emerge between two styles
of discourse: one which pays scrupulous attention to its own prejudices and assumptions, and another
which allows itself to fall into brisk approbations and even brisker dismissals, generally of literary work
which differs in some obvious respect from Prynneâs own. The contradiction is similar to that which was
described in Chapter 2, between Prynneâs high-minded remarks on the role of prose in little magazines
or worksheets such as The English Intelligencer and his actual practice. Yet the fact that there is such
a gap, even between texts produced at roughly the same time, is important. As a necessary response
to centuries of gendered and racialised violence, the prevailing culture of literature and the academic
humanities in the 2010s privileges qualities such as empathy, critical self-examination and attention to
difference. In this context, if all of Prynneâs non-fictional prose was equally vitriolic, it might easily be
written off in toto as lacking in contemporary relevance: less Lewis than Leavis. That it is in fact so
heterogeneous paradoxically extends a saving umbrella over all of it, effectively guaranteeing at least
the possibility of insight in even the most acid texts. It would appear important, thenâif a reliable
understanding of Prynneâs relation to fictional prose is to be reachedâto trace the outline of this gap
over the necessarily vague set of supposedly positive and negative characteristics considered above.

Thus, we can separate a tendency to dismiss writers by implying their exclusion from the category of
sexual maturityââpre-sexualâ, âold grannyââfrom the question of what an aesthetic attachment to
sexual maturity might actually mean. By the same token, to ascribe specific aesthetic qualities to
lazy national stereotypes does not necessarily diminish the value of those qualities attributed to the
speakerâs own nationâin Prynneâs case, soundness, solidity, effectively truth itselfâthough it may be
difficult to disentangle them from the conceptual system in which they are set forth.
In order to provide a final assessment of the conjunction of these qualities, it will be helpful to
return to the most extensive of the non-fictional prose texts mentioned so farâPrynneâs January 1972
letter to Oliver. The description in this text of American poets as âart-gallery nympholeptsâ has already
